  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();

      initializeSiwa();

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer9114066258021722148"><i>The System of the World,</i> the third and concluding volume of Neal Stephenson's shelf-bending Baroque Cycle (<i>Quicksilver</i> and <i>The Confusion</i>), brings the epic historical saga to its thrilling - and truly awe-inspiring - conclusion.<br/><br/>Set in the early 18th century and featuring a diverse cast of characters that includes alchemists, philosophers, mathematicians, spies, thieves, pir</span>
  <span id="freeText9114066258021722148" style="display:none"><i>The System of the World,</i> the third and concluding volume of Neal Stephenson's shelf-bending Baroque Cycle (<i>Quicksilver</i> and <i>The Confusion</i>), brings the epic historical saga to its thrilling - and truly awe-inspiring - conclusion.<br /><br />Set in the early 18th century and featuring a diverse cast of characters that includes alchemists, philosophers, mathematicians, spies, thieves, pirates, and royalty, <i>The System of the World</i> follows Daniel Waterhouse, an unassuming philosopher and confidant to some of the most brilliant minds of the age, as he returns to England to try and repair the rift between geniuses Sir Isaac Newton and Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz. After reluctantly leaving his family in Boston, Waterhouse arrives in England and is almost killed by a mysterious Infernal Device. Having been away from the war-decimated country for two decades, Waterhouse quickly learns that although many things have changed, there is still violent revolution simmering just beneath the surface of seemingly civilized society. With Queen Anne deathly ill and Tories and Whigs jostling for political supremacy, Waterhouse and Newton vow to figure out who is trying to kill certain scientists and decipher the riddle behind the legend of King Solomon's gold, a mythical hoard of precious metal with miraculous properties.<br /><br />Arguably one of the most ambitious -- and most researched -- stories ever written, Stephenson's Baroque Cycle is set in one of the most turbulent and exciting times in human history. Filled with wild adventure, political intrigue, social upheaval, civilization-changing discoveries, cabalistic mysticism, and even a little romance, this massive saga is worth its weight in (Solomon's) gold. <br /><i>Paul Goat Allen</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="9114066258021722148" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_26106900" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26106900" />
    <a title="Kemper" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/405390-kemper"><img alt="Kemper" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495476851p2/405390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/26106900?book_show_action=true">Jul 02, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kemper" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kemper" href="/user/show/405390-kemper">Kemper</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=modern-lit">modern-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26106900" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11026951095285079640"><b>(Excerpt from the journal of Neal Stephenson.)</b><br/><br/>So here I am, trying to wrap up the last book of the <i>The Baroque Cycle</i>. This thing has gotten completely out of control. I knew it’d be huge when I planned it, but this story has sprawled everywhere. What the hell was I thinking? Any one of the story threads I’ve had going could be a fair sized novel in itself. Now I gotta gather them all up and try to come up with some kind of coherent ending. I’m not going to have a fan left if I don’t wrap this up</span>
  <span id="freeText11026951095285079640" style="display:none"><b>(Excerpt from the journal of Neal Stephenson.)</b><br /><br />So here I am, trying to wrap up the last book of the <i>The Baroque Cycle</i>. This thing has gotten completely out of control. I knew it’d be huge when I planned it, but this story has sprawled everywhere. What the hell was I thinking? Any one of the story threads I’ve had going could be a fair sized novel in itself. Now I gotta gather them all up and try to come up with some kind of coherent ending. I’m not going to have a fan left if I don’t wrap this up well.<br /><br />Deep breath. OK, where are we at and what do I need to do to finish this damn thing?<br /><br />* The main story has finally reached 1714 so at least I don’t have to keep flash forwarding to an older Daniel Waterhouse going back to England after leaving Massachusetts. <br /><br />* Daniel is returning to England to try and settle the dispute between Isaac Newton and Leibniz over who invented the calculus, but I want him to get wrapped up in political intrigue about who would succeed Queen Anne.<br /><br />* I also want Daniel to lay some of the groundwork for the upcoming Industrial Revolution by getting involved with the invention of an early mining pump. (Side note: The Engine for Raising Water by Fire sounds cool!)<br /><br />* Time to ramp up all this business about the legendary gold of King Solomon. I also need to tie that back to the ageless Enoch Root. That’s my sci-fi element that’ll keep the geeks turning pages in a European historical fiction.<br /><br />* When I left off, Jack Shaftoe was being blackmailed by the king of France to go to England and destroy confidence in the currency being overseen by the new master of the mint, Isaac Newton. But Jack doesn’t know that Isaac is obsessed with King Solomon’s gold and only took the job so that he can get gold from around the world brought to him to find it. And Isaac doesn’t know that his counterfeiting nemesis Jack already has the Solonomic gold. <br /><br />* Eliza is going to have less to do in this one, but I’ll have her increasing her anti-slavery efforts.<br /><br />* I’m going to let Daniel and Isaac Newton play detective when trying to track down Jack by giving some fun history of how people used to have to do their own prosecutions and hire ‘thief-takers’ to track down criminals.<br /><br />* It’ll be a cool twist late in the book when Daniel goes from hunting criminals to plotting some major criminal projects.<br /><br />* Shit. I’ve also got to work in the stuff about Leibniz and Daniel trying to build a ‘logic mill’ for Peter the Great in Russia. Can I work the gold into that? Make it so that everyone is trying to get their hands on it?<br /><br />* Some random things I want to include also include a duel between two men fought with cannons instead of pistols, clockwork phosphorus bombs, an assault on the Tower of London and a coach chase through the streets. <br /><br />* I also got to give the readers some idea of the fate of the approximately 1137 supporting characters I’ve introduced in this.<br /><br />Damn, I’ve got my work cut out for me. Fortunately, I’m a genius so it shouldn’t be a problem. But I’ll give my fans a break after this. No big giant books with multiple plot lines. Something short and simple. Although I do have this idea for a new language……</span>
  <a data-text-id="11026951095285079640" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2209217583" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2209217583" />
    <a title="Darwin8u" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u"><img alt="Darwin8u" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547440402p2/2294090.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2209217583?book_show_action=true">Dec 09, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Darwin8u" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Darwin8u" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u">Darwin8u</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=scifi">scifi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=finance">finance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=economics">economics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2209217583" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4175121289772429988"><i>“It has been my view for some years that a new System of the World is being created around us. I used to suppose that it would drive out and annihilate any older Systems. But things I have seen recently, in the subterranean places beneath the Bank, have convinced me that new Systems never replace old ones, but only surround and encapsulate them, even as, under a microscope, we may see that living within our bodies are animalcules, smaller and simpler than us, and yet thriving even as we thrive."</i></span>
  <span id="freeText4175121289772429988" style="display:none"><i>“It has been my view for some years that a new System of the World is being created around us. I used to suppose that it would drive out and annihilate any older Systems. But things I have seen recently, in the subterranean places beneath the Bank, have convinced me that new Systems never replace old ones, but only surround and encapsulate them, even as, under a microscope, we may see that living within our bodies are animalcules, smaller and simpler than us, and yet thriving even as we thrive."</i><br>-- Neal Stephenson, The System of the World<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1543293727i/26653270._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="307" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>Solomon's Gold (The Baroque Cycle, Vol. 3, Book 1)</b> <br><br><i>“On the contrary, my lord...there is nothing quite so civilized as to be recognized in public places as the author of books no one has read.”</i> <br>- Neal Stephenson, The Baroque Cycle, Vol 3, Book 1<br><br>I can feel the end of this series closing in. The sixth book of this series, nested, like a Russian doll inside of Volume 3 (The System of the World) centers primarily on Daniel Waterhouse. Daniel has been summoned back to England to act as a middle-man (or a narrative bridge?) between Isaac Newton and Gottfried Leibniz concerning the invention of Calculus. Someone tries to kill him with an infernal device (mechanical bomb). The book ends with Jack Shaftoe (aka Jack the Coiner) attempting a heist of the Tower of London where Netwon is the "Warden" and later "Master" of the Royal Mint. Newton has been using this role at the Royal Mint to standardize the guinea, but also to to search for Solomon's lost gold. <br><br>The book tends to bend easily between swashbuckling adventure and nerdy historical/light scifi fiction. It is dense in parts, but it is hard to not respect Stephenson's ability to weave the real with the almost supernatural and the outrageous. I'm constantly entertained by The Baroque Cycle but the charm is starting to tarnish a bit and I'm ready for this almost literary adventure/ride <br>to end.<br><br><b>Currency (The Baroque Cycle, Vol. 3, Book 2)</b> <br><br><i>“For most of the day and night, time oppresses me. It is only when I am at work on the innards of a clock-or a lock-that time stops."</i><br>- Neal Stephenson, The Currency<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1543130453i/26645207._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="177" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Stephenson continues the last volume (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116257.The_System_of_the_World__The_Baroque_Cycle___3_" title="The System of the World (The Baroque Cycle, #3) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">The System of the World</a>) of his Baroque trilogy with Book 7: "Currency". Like in Book 6, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2017.Solomon_s_Gold__The_Baroque_Cycle__Vol__3__Book_1_" title="Solomon's Gold (The Baroque Cycle, Vol. 3, Book 1) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Solomon's Gold</a>, "Currency" is primarily focused on Daniel Waterhouse trying to track down Jack Shaftoe (or Jack the Counterfeiter) who is making England's money financially dubious by messing with the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trial_of_the_Pyx" rel="nofollow noopener">Pyx</a> (and hence putting ALL of England's currency at risk). Isaac Newton is helping Daniel Waterhouse track down Jack, both because as the Master of the Mint his reputation (and head) are at risk. But he is also motivated because as an alchemist he suspects that Jack Shaftoe has some of Solomon's gold. While all of this is going on Eliza is trying to help Princess Caroline survive the inevitable succession issues that will develop (including assassination attempts) once Queen Anne dies.<br><br>This has probably been the least "exciting" of the novels, but like any long work (eventually, the Baroque Cycle will clock in at about 2650 pages) there are bound to be parts of a work that float down the narrative current rather than quant down. Still, I did enjoy it.<br><br><b>The System of the World (The Baroque Cycle, Vol. 3, Book 3)</b><br><br><i>““We are all up to something”</i> <br>- Neal Stephenson, The Baroque Cycle, Vol 3, Book 3<br><br>This is the end. Beautiful end. Assayed. The end. The eighth and final book of this series, which also shares the same name as the final volume of this series: (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116257.The_System_of_the_World__The_Baroque_Cycle___3_" title="The System of the World (The Baroque Cycle, #3) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">The System of the World</a>). This final book in an eight book series is driven largely by two large and parallel events (the corronation of King George is a mere distraction). First, the hanging of Jack Shaftoe. Second, the Trial of the Pyx (and by proxy, a trial of Sir Isaac Newton). There are other events: the spiriting away of Solomon's gold, the escape of Jack's sons and Dappa (the First mate of the Minerva who ends up caught in a funky antislavery campaign against Charles White (one of the many villians of the book), the death of Roger Comstock (and other deaths ane ressurrections).<br><br>Anyway, I enjoyed how Stephenson wrapped this up. It is weird thinking that these three volumes:<br>1. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/823.Quicksilver__The_Baroque_Cycle___1_" title="Quicksilver (The Baroque Cycle, #1) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Quicksilver</a><br>2. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/822.The_Confusion__The_Baroque_Cycle___2_" title="The Confusion (The Baroque Cycle, #2) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Confusion</a><br>3. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116257.The_System_of_the_World__The_Baroque_Cycle___3_" title="The System of the World (The Baroque Cycle, #3) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">The System of the World</a><br><br>are all essentially prequels to: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/816.Cryptonomicon" title="Cryptonomicon by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Cryptonomicon</a>. I enjoyed the dance. It might have been one volume too much. Reading Stephenson, some days, does feel a bit like <i>Peine forte et dure</i>. How about just one more volume? That said, I did read all of the diaries of Samuel Pepys, so I am a glutton for the English Restoration period. I found this a fantastic (often literally FANTASTIC) way of examining the period and systems of science and religion and politics during this period. Obviously, much of the specifics are fiction, but many of the things floating like <i>mouches volantes</i> are grounded in facts. Sometimes, the best way to learn history is not to read it, but to play with it a bit; bend it and examine it under unusual lights and in different heats.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4175121289772429988" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_6380958" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6380958" />
    <a title="Ben" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/390823-ben"><img alt="Ben" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6380958?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ben" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ben" href="/user/show/390823-ben">Ben</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">People who have read Cryptonomicon</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/390823-ben?shelf=pleased-to-have-read">pleased-to-have-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6380958" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2336534514642052089">I am doing this as a review for the Baroque cycle altogether, so don't bother reading the reviews for the other two if you are reading this one.<br/><br/>The Baroque cycle is a massive, epic, depressingly wide reaching body of creative work which, I believe, has made several well respected fantasy/sci-fi novelists give up and go home. If it hasn't, it definitely should. It's just so.... big. And while there are a lot of authors who have written large things (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34.The_Fellowship_of_the_Ring__The_Lord_of_the_Rings__Part_1_' title='The Fellowship of the Ring (The Lord of the Rings, Part 1) by J.R.R. Tolkien' rel='nofollow noopener'>the Lord of the Rings</a>, the Wheel of Time, a Son</span>
  <span id="freeText2336534514642052089" style="display:none">I am doing this as a review for the Baroque cycle altogether, so don't bother reading the reviews for the other two if you are reading this one.<br /><br />The Baroque cycle is a massive, epic, depressingly wide reaching body of creative work which, I believe, has made several well respected fantasy/sci-fi novelists give up and go home. If it hasn't, it definitely should. It's just so.... big. And while there are a lot of authors who have written large things (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34.The_Fellowship_of_the_Ring__The_Lord_of_the_Rings__Part_1_" title="The Fellowship of the Ring (The Lord of the Rings, Part 1) by J.R.R. Tolkien" rel="nofollow noopener">the Lord of the Rings</a>, the Wheel of Time, a Song of Fire and Ice, etc., etc., etc.) most of them at some point get a little bogged down, lose their way, can't keep track of what everyone's doing, or just get so damn large in scope that they write a thousand pages and nothing happens. The Baroque cycle never runs into any of those things. Really it reads kind of like a single 4,000 page novel. And it's so damn clever! Definitely don't read it before reading Cryptonomicon, despite the fact that the story comes first chronologically. There is a damn good reason Stephenson wrote the books in the order he did. For more on this, see the Narnia books. It will take you a long time to read, there will be moments when you just can't believe you read parts of it right, but if you stick with it you will not be disappointed. Personally gauranteed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2336534514642052089" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_69576344" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/69576344" />
    <a title="Steve" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/827856-steve"><img alt="Steve" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1255806840p2/827856.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/69576344?book_show_action=true">Aug 31, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve" href="/user/show/827856-steve">Steve</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer69576344" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15177521236818763828">Well, I'm now officially depressed. I finished reading the Baroque Cycle. To say that I enjoyed reading the series would be to stretch the word "enjoyed" to the breaking point. It would be rolling the word "enjoyed" off to the juicing room. It would be hanging the word "enjoyed" until half dead, and then drawing and quartering the word "enjoyed" by four sturdy teams of horses, in the hopes that somewhere in the process "enjoyed" would choose to reveal the location of its ringleader, a much more </span>
  <span id="freeText15177521236818763828" style="display:none">Well, I'm now officially depressed. I finished reading the Baroque Cycle. To say that I enjoyed reading the series would be to stretch the word "enjoyed" to the breaking point. It would be rolling the word "enjoyed" off to the juicing room. It would be hanging the word "enjoyed" until half dead, and then drawing and quartering the word "enjoyed" by four sturdy teams of horses, in the hopes that somewhere in the process "enjoyed" would choose to reveal the location of its ringleader, a much more powerful verb, more thoroughly capable of conveying the intricacies of the action. One might imagine a brutal member of the Spanish inquisition leaning over the word "enjoyed", asking it "Who taught you these heresies? Was it 'relished'? 'Savored'? Perhaps it was 'adored'?" Here the inquisitor looks down at his documents, "My friend Thesaurus suggests that you work for 'cherished'! Is it 'cherished'?!"<br /><br />Lest I belabor the point a bit too long, I'll suffice to say that the series kept me absolutely enthralled, this last book somehow doing this with greater fervor than its predecessors, and move to the reasons that this was so:<br /><br />1. What I like to affectionately label "Holy Shit Moments". The definition of such a moment is simple: When the moment comes, the audience (in this case, the reader), is likely to exclaim "Holy shit!" or some similar outburst. System of the World is full of them, and they are a diverse lot. <br /><br />2. This book has something for everyone. While perhaps less so than in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/822.The_Confusion__The_Baroque_Cycle___2_" title="The Confusion (The Baroque Cycle, #2) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Confusion</a>, there were buckles to be swashed. There was much political intrigue. This is true in the pure sense of some truly epic politicking between the Tories and the Whigs, but also in more colloquial Survivor-secret-alliance senses as well. The general trend of scientific and philosophical endeavors continued in this book as well. There's romance, action, mystery, very much in abundance.<br /><br />3. Rewarding payoffs. You look for these towards the end of a series. Lots of loose ends to be tied up nicely, lots of characters who need their minor (inconsequential?) arcs tied up. Jerks that need to get killed (you hope). This book ties up the series pretty darn flawlessly, with positive resolutions and negative resolutions distributed amongst the various plotlines in generally pleasing ways, albeit at times in predictable ones, if you are familiar with the historical period. <br /><br />4. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/545.Neal_Stephenson" title="Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Neal Stephenson</a> is very observant, and has a way of utilizing these observations to great effect. A common praise giving to stand up comedians is that "he's saying what we're all thinking", due to their ability to point out the oddities of society so well. Stephenson's ability to do so (not always for humorous purposes) is phenomenal. <br /><br />I've already got one person to add the Baroque cycle to their to-read list, and I've got a non good reads user started as well (with two more in the wings). I think this series is right up there with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13496.A_Game_of_Thrones__A_Song_of_Ice_and_Fire___1_" title="A Game of Thrones (A Song of Ice and Fire, #1) by George R.R. Martin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Game of Thrones</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15177.Bridge_of_Birds_A_Novel_of_an_Ancient_China_That_Never_Was" title="Bridge of Birds A Novel of an Ancient China That Never Was by Barry Hughart" rel="nofollow noopener">Bridge of Birds</a> now as my go to recommendation. It's that good. Read it. Read it now.<br /><br />(starting with these quotes):<br /><br />"Mr. Threader was a meat tabula rasa, like the exposed cliff of a roast beef left by the carver's knife" (p. 18). If you can think of a better way to describe someone as nondescript, I'd like to hear it.<br /><br />"How many sheep in England? And not just in January 1714, but in all the millennia before? Why had the island not sunk into the sea under the weight of sheep-bones and sheep-teeth? Possibly because their wool was exported--mostly to Holland--<i>which was in fact sinking into the sea!</i> Q.E.D." (p. 20-1).<br /><br />"All salvaged, not because they had innate value, but because they'd been given to the Royal Society by important people. They'd been kept here just as a young couple keeps the ugly wedding present from the rich aunt" (p. 64).<br /><br />"'Its badness is proof of my sincerity,' Roger said modestly. 'If I wrote her an <i>excellent</i> love-poem, it might be said of me, that I had done it only to flaunt my wit'" (p. 71-2).<br /><br />"Then he got a look on his face as if he were thinking. Daniel had learned, in his almost seventy years, not to expect much of people who got such looks, because thinking really was something one ought to do all the time" (p. 168).<br /><br />"Which only went to show that Englishmen could live anywhere. Condemn an Englishman to hell, and he'd plant a bed of petunias and roll out a nice bowling-green on the brimstone" (p. 200).<br /><br />"If Daniel and Pie were close together both in position and velocity, then pie-eating became a practical, and tempting, possibility. If Pie were far asunder from Daniel or moving at a large relative velocity--e.g. being hurled at his face--then its pie-ness was somehow impaired, at least from the Daniel frame of reference" (p. 457).<br /><br />"Myself, I am comfortable with the notion that we are Machines made of Meat, and there's no more free will in us than there is in a cuckoo-clock, and that the spirit, soul, or whatever you want to call it, is a faery-tale" (p. 679).<br /><br />"But that was one of those errands that, if not achieved in the first twelve hours, would remain undone centuries later. And, as all of this was shewing, the fetching-out of these three items had long since ossified into a ceremony" (p. 830).<br /><br />"The Old Testament [reading:] is a length of black grosgrain ribbon that takes him into the type of passage whose sole purpose, in a Christian service, is to demonstrate just how much trouble we would all be in, if we were still Jews" (p. 833).<br /><br />"To be hanged bu the neck until dead is one thing; but to be forced to listen to a reading from the Old Testament <i>twice</i>, why, that is not only Unusual but Cruel" (p. 834).</span>
  <a data-text-id="15177521236818763828" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText9083547213446255928" style="display:none">finished the reread of System of the World and I won't add too much beyond what i wrote in 2008 when i first read the series; less flamboyant and mostly following a 67-68 year old Daniel back in England for the momentous year 1714, but with lots of twists and turns and great appearances from Jack and Eliza<br /><br /> (review on first read 2008) Superb ending - in all senses of the word - to the Baroque trilogy and a must for people who love historical fiction a la Dumas or D. Dunnett. The light sf-nal elements of the trilogy disappointed purists, but so what - the modern world which Mr. Stephenson accurately in my opinion claims that started with Newton and Leibniz is as sf-nal and improbable as any sf novel, so this extraordinary description in 3000 pages of that beginning is a masterpiece.<br /><br /> And of course we get to say farewell to Jack, Eliza, and all the characters in a true historical romance fashion...</span>

<div id="review_3214858015" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3214858015" />
    <a title="Judy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3333943-judy"><img alt="Judy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1267037102p2/3333943.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3214858015?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Judy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Judy" href="/user/show/3333943-judy">Judy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=21st-century-fiction">21st-century-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=set-in-great-britain">set-in-great-britain</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3214858015" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12518551183477719989"><br/>Last spring, Neal Stephenson's latest novel, <i>Fall, or, Dodge in Hell</i> was released. I bought the hardcover right away ( I always buy his books), but it sat on the shelf. <br/><br/>I am a committed fan of this author. I read <i>Snow Crash</i>, his third book, in 2004 and was impressed! <br/><br/>Great characters, exciting plot. Since I had not yet read William Gibson, I thought it was he who had invented cyber-punk. Actually, as it turns out, they both did. In 1984!<br/><br/>I went on to read <i>The Diamond Age</i> and <i>Cryptonomicon</i>. Always</span>
  <span id="freeText12518551183477719989" style="display:none"><br />Last spring, Neal Stephenson's latest novel, <i>Fall, or, Dodge in Hell</i> was released. I bought the hardcover right away ( I always buy his books), but it sat on the shelf. <br /><br />I am a committed fan of this author. I read <i>Snow Crash</i>, his third book, in 2004 and was impressed! <br /><br />Great characters, exciting plot. Since I had not yet read William Gibson, I thought it was he who had invented cyber-punk. Actually, as it turns out, they both did. In 1984!<br /><br />I went on to read <i>The Diamond Age</i> and <i>Cryptonomicon</i>. Always lagging a decade behind. Both were amazing.<br /> <br />In 2008, Books Expo America was held in Los Angeles. I got to meet Neal in person. He is a tiny, short, rail thin man with a beard, an elf! I picked up an ARC of <i>Anathem</i>, released 9/2008. He signed my copy. He seemed quite the introvert who practiced social distancing as a life style though he has always kept a strong web presence.<br /> <br />I have still not read Anathem, but was inspired by meeting him and began his Baroque Cycle. I read <i>Quicksilver</i> and was exhilarated to find descendants of Cryptonomicon characters Jack Shaftoe and Daniel Waterhouse, not to mention Enoch Root, back in the late 1600s. Also I met my favorite female character ever, Eliza of Qwghlm.<br /><br />If you have not read these books, I may have lost you by now. If you have, I hope you are reliving the wonder of it all.<br /><br />The Baroque Cycle consists of three volumes, each of which is well over 800 pages. Set in the late 1600s and early 1700s in England, France and all over the known world in those times, the books trace the transformation of Europe away from the Dark Ages and into rational, scientific systems of government and finance.<br /><br />That might sound ponderous and boring. It is not! The Thirty Years War, the discovery of calculus by Isaac Newton and Gotfried Leibniz, the effects of alchemy on science, the explosion of gold and slavery due to the expansion of the New World, and more, are given the Alexander Dumas (Count of Monte Cristo) treatment. <br /><br />A mind boggling cast of characters engage in unlimited adventures: Kings and Queens, Dukes and Lords, pirates, Royal Society geeks and The King of the Vagabonds, Jack Shaftoe fill the pages. By the end of the three books the system we now spend time navigating and fighting, that is Banking, has been born.<br /><br />As I finished my reading year of 2019, feeling like I had run and won a marathon by reading 156 books, I caught my breath and determined to read in 2020 as many as I could of the long, I mean really long, books I had been putting off. Forget quantity. Read those tomes.<br /><br />So, in January I reread Quicksilver. I did love it the first time but did not think I had entirely understood it. I have to thank the late Dorothy Dunnett and the two of her intricate historical novels I read not too long ago (The Game of Kings and Queen's Play), for showing me how to read such things. In fact, Neal acknowledges her as an influence.<br /><br />In February, I reread the second book of the Baroque Cycle, <i>The Confusion</i>, about which I recalled nothing but feeling confused after reading it in 2012. I am pretty sure I assimilated it this time.<br /><br />My conclusion after the second reading: Many people these days think or worry that the world is getting worse. I think human beings on Earth have always led a mostly insane course, with a few who work towards acquiring knowledge as a means of creating a just civilization. What we see going on now is still following both of those trajectories.<br /><br />This month I read, for the first time, the third volume, <i>The System of the World</i>. I was for it ready now. All immersed in the history and the characters, I was dying to find out if Newton and Liebniz would ever resolve their differences, if Jack and Eliza would ever make up, and what would become of the long suffering Daniel Waterhouse.<br /><br />The conflicts and plots and mostly gruesome adventures of these characters continue in this volume without respite. Queen Anne of England (did you watch "The Favourite" last year?) meets her demise and is succeeded by King George I. Great Britain, Europe and the world will move forward and never be the same. The System of the World, as we now know it, has been born. <br /><br />As for Eliza, Jack, Newton and Leibniz, you will have to read the books yourself.<br /><br />On this 14th day of March, 2020, as the world stays home and watches pantries empty, gets bored, tries to quell anxiety as best we can, I give you probably the longest post I have ever written. I hope it has helped fill some time for you.<br /><br />If you have too much time on your hands, all of the Neal Stephenson books I have mentioned are available as ebooks and audio books, not to mention real books. You will be whisked away to times much different than ours and yet feel rather at home. The great conflict between reason and madness continues. <br /><br />I look forward to reading that signed copy of Anathem in April.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12518551183477719989" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/9963442" />
    <a title="Amy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/634536-amy"><img alt="Amy" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/9963442?book_show_action=true">Dec 04, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amy" href="/user/show/634536-amy">Amy</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer9963442" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5592575419850305955">This series was an ambitious project on Stephenson's part, but I think he tried to do too much. I liked the characters he created and found the plot interesting, however, the books are uneven in their pacing and sort of unfocused. Sometimes it's a love story, sometimes it's an adventure, sometimes it's a mystery. He does the love and adventure well, but really falls down on the mystery aspect. It's as though he randomly decided to make things obscure for no real reason. He also just takes too da</span>
  <span id="freeText5592575419850305955" style="display:none">This series was an ambitious project on Stephenson's part, but I think he tried to do too much. I liked the characters he created and found the plot interesting, however, the books are uneven in their pacing and sort of unfocused. Sometimes it's a love story, sometimes it's an adventure, sometimes it's a mystery. He does the love and adventure well, but really falls down on the mystery aspect. It's as though he randomly decided to make things obscure for no real reason. He also just takes too damn long to get to the point in too many parts. I kept being highly interested in what was going to happen to the characters but frustrated at how unnecessarily long he took to develop any one story line. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5592575419850305955" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karl" href="/user/show/315295-karl">Karl</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26714611" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10310335690273937369">I don't even know how to begin to review this trilogy. It's really all one novel, and so it might then be the longest novel I've read.<br/><br/>It has everything. An around the world sea voyage. The Barbary corsairs. Love triumphing over death. Women trimuphing over men. The beginnings of the Enlightenment. Battles. The formation of the monetary system. A duel with unconventional firearms. Blackbeard. Peter the Great. And a gaggle of mathematicians.<br/><br/>Extensively researched historical fiction, I've been hard</span>
  <span id="freeText10310335690273937369" style="display:none">I don't even know how to begin to review this trilogy. It's really all one novel, and so it might then be the longest novel I've read.<br /><br />It has everything. An around the world sea voyage. The Barbary corsairs. Love triumphing over death. Women trimuphing over men. The beginnings of the Enlightenment. Battles. The formation of the monetary system. A duel with unconventional firearms. Blackbeard. Peter the Great. And a gaggle of mathematicians.<br /><br />Extensively researched historical fiction, I've been hard pressed to find anything that isn't accurate when I read more about these people and these times. I have no idea how Stephenson kept that all in his head.<br /><br />But thank goodness he did.</span>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3389268303?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alan" href="/user/show/1153204-alan">Alan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">&quot;footpads {...}, file-clys, night-gamesters, running-smoblers, or till-divers&quot; (pp. 718-719)</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Alan by: </span>
      Prior alarums and excursions
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3389268303" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12738721288253585843">And so at long last we come to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116257.The_System_of_the_World__The_Baroque_Cycle___3_' title='The System of the World (The Baroque Cycle, #3) by Neal Stephenson' rel='nofollow noopener'>The System of the World</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/545.Neal_Stephenson' title='Neal Stephenson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Neal Stephenson</a>'s resounding conclusion to the <a href='https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Baroque_Cycle' rel='nofollow noopener'>Baroque Cycle</a>,<br/><strong>IN WHICH</strong> we discover that this Daniel Waterhouse chap may be more central to the narrative than first appeared.<br/><br/>I suppose we can be forgiven for coming so late to this understanding, especially after the back-and-forth shell game Stephenson played with us in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/822.The_Confusion__The_Baroque_Cycle___2_' title='The Confusion (The Baroque Cycle, #2) by Neal Stephenson' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Confusion</a>, but the older, bolder and maybe even wiser Waterhouse we see in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116257.The_System_of_the_World__The_Baroque_Cycle___3_' title='The System of the World (The Baroque Cycle, #3) by Neal Stephenson' rel='nofollow noopener'>The System of the World</a> certainly seems to hav</span>
  <span id="freeText12738721288253585843" style="display:none">And so at long last we come to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116257.The_System_of_the_World__The_Baroque_Cycle___3_" title="The System of the World (The Baroque Cycle, #3) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">The System of the World</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/545.Neal_Stephenson" title="Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Neal Stephenson</a>'s resounding conclusion to the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Baroque_Cycle" rel="nofollow noopener">Baroque Cycle</a>,<br /><strong>IN WHICH</strong> we discover that this Daniel Waterhouse chap may be more central to the narrative than first appeared.<br /><br />I suppose we can be forgiven for coming so late to this understanding, especially after the back-and-forth shell game Stephenson played with us in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/822.The_Confusion__The_Baroque_Cycle___2_" title="The Confusion (The Baroque Cycle, #2) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Confusion</a>, but the older, bolder and maybe even wiser Waterhouse we see in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116257.The_System_of_the_World__The_Baroque_Cycle___3_" title="The System of the World (The Baroque Cycle, #3) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">The System of the World</a> certainly seems to have become something of—shall we say—an <em>influencer</em>, whether he's bantering with nobility:<blockquote>"There is a sickness of the mind that comes over those who bide too long in London, which causes otherwise rational men to put forced and absurd meanings on events that are accidental."<br />"I have observed that sickness in full flower," Daniel allowed, thinking of one man in particular.<br />—Daniel Waterhouse, in conversation with the Earl of Lostwithiel, p.5</blockquote>Or musing on his own ever-increasing weight of experience:<blockquote>Once, he had feared that old age would bring senility; now, he was certain it would slowly paralyze him by encumbering each tiny thing with all sorts of significations.<br />—p.16</blockquote>That's a familiar feeling for me as well, these days.<br /><br />Of course, <em>other</em> important characters get some good lines too...<blockquote>"That God hears the prayers of Lutherans, is a proposition hotly disputed by many, including many Lutherans."<br />—From a letter sent by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" rel="nofollow noopener">Gottfried Leibniz</a> to Daniel Waterhouse, p. 51</blockquote><br /><br />Make no mistake—Stephenson hasn't forgotten about Jack and Eliza either. Quite the contrary; our favorite long-distance relationship keeps getting closer (one could even say <em>asymptotically</em> closer) throughout <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116257.The_System_of_the_World__The_Baroque_Cycle___3_" title="The System of the World (The Baroque Cycle, #3) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">The System of the World</a>, and much of the action still revolves around Jack's efforts to prove himself worthy of his lady love despite having been forced into the rôle of England's greatest criminal mastermind.<br /><br />The alternating narratives of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/822.The_Confusion__The_Baroque_Cycle___2_" title="The Confusion (The Baroque Cycle, #2) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Confusion</a> have been replaced with a more straightforward progression of events, and a greater focus on the scientific and intellectual changes being wrought on European society. The old system of monarchs and vassals bound together by faith and force of arms is being displaced (not without effort) by an entirely <em>new</em> System of the World, based on intangible flows of money, of influence, and of ideas: in short, of <em>data.</em><br /><br />And, as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116257.The_System_of_the_World__The_Baroque_Cycle___3_" title="The System of the World (The Baroque Cycle, #3) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">The System of the World</a> draws closer to the Industrial Revolution, we also draw closer (one might even say <em>asymptotically</em> closer) to the subjects Stephenson addresses in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/816.Cryptonomicon" title="Cryptonomicon by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Cryptonomicon</a>.<br /><br />For example, that same letter from Leibniz to Waterhouse quoted above also contains this philosophickal meditation (which may even be historical, for all I know) on smoke rings...<br /><blockquote>I saw one or two smoke-rings, about the size of a man's hat, propagating across the room, and retaining their shape and <em>vis viva</em> for extraordinary distances. These rings are unlike water-waves, which consist of different water at different times, for smoke rings propagate through clear air, proving that they indeed carry their own substance with them, neither diluting it with, nor dispersing it into, the surrounding atmosphere. And yet there is nothing special about the smoke as such—it is the same smoke that hangs over battlefields in shapeless clouds. The identity of a smoke ring would appear to consist, not in the stuff of which it is made, for that is commonplace and indifferent, bur rather in a particular set of relationships that is brought into being among its parts. It is this pattern of relationships that coheres in space and persists in time and endows the smoke-ring with an identity. Perhaps some similar observation might be made about other entities that we observe, and credit with uniqueness and identity, including even human beings.<br />—p.54</blockquote><br /><br />The tantalizing might-have-beens of mechanical computation also come in for scrutiny. There's a close kinship, for example, between the "Logic Mill" that Waterhouse and Leibniz are working on and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1807288.The_Difference_Engine" title="The Difference Engine by William Gibson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Difference Engine</a> in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/9226.William_Gibson" title="William Gibson" rel="nofollow noopener">William Gibson</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/34429.Bruce_Sterling" title="Bruce Sterling" rel="nofollow noopener">Bruce Sterling</a>'s fascinating alternative past.<br /><br />Leibniz' Mill, though it was never built, also subtly influences the passages featuring Dappa, one of Jack Shaftoe's fellow galley slaves, a Black man whose witty broadsides, penned from prison (he is in jail for possessing stolen property—that is, <em>himself</em>) help accomplish the abolition of the British slave trade. Dappa argues articulately and passionately for his freedom, in print and in person, in English and French, and yet one smug Tory still says,<blockquote>"One would suppose there would be no point in holding a conversation with a man who does not understand what he is saying. And yet he described yesterday's weather better than I could!"<br />—Peer, pp. 168-169</blockquote>This toff's beliefs, so obviously wrong, do double duty for Stephenson—not only does Peer's boorish behavior (he's actually talking <em>with</em> Dappa when he says this!) contrast with Dappa's intelligence and graciousness, it also seems clearly intended as a parallel to <em>modern</em> skepticism about the possibility of <em>artificial</em> intelligence, a skepticism exemplified by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/29174.John_Rogers_Searle" title="John Rogers Searle" rel="nofollow noopener">John Searle</a>'s famous thought experiment on the <a href="https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/chinese-room/" rel="nofollow noopener">"Chinese Room"</a> (or see the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_room" rel="nofollow noopener">Wikipedia article</a> on the subject—although be warned, both of those links go to rather long reads themselves).<br /><br /><br />Amid all this philosophical discourse, though, Stephenson still manages to slip a lot of humor into <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116257.The_System_of_the_World__The_Baroque_Cycle___3_" title="The System of the World (The Baroque Cycle, #3) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">The System of the World</a>. For one dry (heh) example:<blockquote>Orney might be oblivious to rain, but, anticipating that the others would whinge about it, he had pitched a tarpaulin over <em>Prudence</em>'s midships. This was waterproof except along the seams; wherever anyone touched it; where it had been patched; round any of its constellations of moth-holes; and wherever else it happened to leak.<br />—p.476</blockquote><br /><br />Or this lively back-and-forth:<blockquote>"What have you told the proprietor about who we are, and what we are doing?" Mr. Threader was asking Saturn.<br />"That you are Royal Society men making observations of the daily currency of the river."<br />"He's not going to believe <em>that</em>, is he?"<br />"You didn't ask me what he <em>believes</em>. You asked me what I <em>told</em> him. What he <em>believes</em>, is that you are City men investigating a case of insurance fraud by spying on a certain ship anchored out there in the Pool."<br />"Fine—our true purpose shall not be suspected as long as he is telling people <em>that</em>."<br />"Oh no, he's not <em>telling</em> people that. He's <em>telling</em> them that you are a Sect of Dissenters forced to meet in secret because of the recent passage of Bolingbroke's Schism Act."<br />"Let the blokes in the tap-room think we are Dissenters then, is all I'm trying to say."<br />"That's not what they think. They think that you are Sodomites," Partry said. This silenced Threader for awhile.<br />—pp.485-486</blockquote><br /><br />From sly observations that remain topical today:<blockquote>"The profession of politics would be altogether too disagreeable," Roger allowed, "without compensations above and beyond what is strictly appropriate."<br />—Roger Comstock, p.539</blockquote><br /><br />To not-so-sly expostulations that... remain topical today:<blockquote>"Look—look at this opera house! Built on the edge of the world by frostbitten shepherds—yet in its size, its glory, truly a monster, an abomination, only possible because of the unnatural distortions that Money has wreaked on the world. The same is true of all London! It should all burn."<br />—Edouard de Gex, p.569</blockquote><br /><br /><blockquote>Another thing that Daniel had been learning was that even if real estate was expensive, people were cheap. Which ought to have been obvious to him from that, in exchange for tiny bits of silver, people were forever shinnying up chimneys, climbing into bed with syphilitics, or taking musket-balls in Belgium. But like most who did not do such things, he went out of his way not to dwell on it, and had quite put it out of his mind until it was brought to his notice<em>{...}</em>.<br />—pp.741-742</blockquote><br /><br /><br />As caught up in the story as I was, I was able to forgive the (very) occasional typographical and proofreading errors inevitable in a book of this length. Mostly. This one did stand out to me, though...<br /><blockquote>Mr. Kikin was amused by the blank expressions on the faces of Orney and Kikin.<br />—p.480</blockquote>That second "Kikin" was most assuredly supposed to have been "Threader," the same one in that discussion with Saturn, above.<br /><br /><br />You may be able to tell, from the excerpts I've chosen to include, that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116257.The_System_of_the_World__The_Baroque_Cycle___3_" title="The System of the World (The Baroque Cycle, #3) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">The System of the World</a> goes all over the <em>mental</em> map—in contrast to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/822.The_Confusion__The_Baroque_Cycle___2_" title="The Confusion (The Baroque Cycle, #2) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Confusion</a>'s decidedly more <em>physical</em> wanderings. But now we've come to the end, of the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Baroque_Cycle" rel="nofollow noopener">Baroque Cycle</a>, and of this review, though not of course of history itself.<br /><br />I will not tell you <em>how</em> it ends, but I think Stephenson really did wrap things up very well (which has not always been the case for his work), and I am very glad to have been able to devote the time and attention required to reread this sprawling, chaotic, wonderful series.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12738721288253585843" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_8024530" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/8024530" />
    <a title="Nicholas Whyte" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/565673-nicholas-whyte"><img alt="Nicholas Whyte" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1261743261p2/565673.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/8024530?book_show_action=true">Oct 21, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicholas Whyte" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicholas Whyte" href="/user/show/565673-nicholas-whyte">Nicholas Whyte</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099463368" href="/book/show/920429.The_System_of_the_World">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer8024530" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3460762012627007586"><a href='http://nhw.livejournal.com/737376.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>http://nhw.livejournal.com/737376.html</a><br/><br/>Any Man, when he shall have completed a Taſk, be it one which he has aſsigned to Himſelf, or an Impoſition from ſome <i>external</i> Party, may experience a certain Euphoria. I write here of two such Taſks which have been completed, <i>videlicet</i>, <i>primo</i>, the Exertions of Master STEPHENSON in writing the Series of Romances, commencing with <b>Cryptonomicon</b> and continued in <b>Quickſilver</b>, <b>The Confuſion</b>, and the Volume here under Conſideration; and <i>secundo</i>, my own Expenditure </span>
  <span id="freeText3460762012627007586" style="display:none"><a href="http://nhw.livejournal.com/737376.html" rel="nofollow noopener">http://nhw.livejournal.com/737376.html</a><br /><br />Any Man, when he shall have completed a Taſk, be it one which he has aſsigned to Himſelf, or an Impoſition from ſome <i>external</i> Party, may experience a certain Euphoria. I write here of two such Taſks which have been completed, <i>videlicet</i>, <i>primo</i>, the Exertions of Master STEPHENSON in writing the Series of Romances, commencing with <b>Cryptonomicon</b> and continued in <b>Quickſilver</b>, <b>The Confuſion</b>, and the Volume here under Conſideration; and <i>secundo</i>, my own Expenditure of Time, Money, Energy and Loſt Sleep in reading them.<br /><br />It has oft been obſerved that I am a Swift Reader. It is my Wont or Habit to complete the Peruſal of a Volume, whoſe Pages may number Ten-Score or thereabouts, in two Nights of Reading in my Bed; or if it be Saturday or the Lord's Day, to read two or three ſuch over the Week-End. Even <b>The Brothers Karamazov</b>, that Renowned Tale penned by the Ruſsian Savant DOSTOIEFFSKY, detained me only a Week, though its Pages number more than a Thouſand. Completing my Study of <b>The Syſtem of the World</b> has required near a Fort'night; yet it is more than an Hundred Pages ſhorter than the Ruſsian Work. I confeſs, I would fain have left the Book untouched upon my Book-Shelf ſome months longer, but was Stirred to read it by my <a href="http://nhw.livejournal.com/723950.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Compariſon</a> of the unread Volumes in my Library with thoſe marked as "unread" by the Clients of <a href="http://www.librarything.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Master SPALDING's <i>Electronick Catalog</i></a>.<br /><br />Even the ſympathetic Reader of Master STEPHENSON's works muſt ſurely wiſh that ſome-body in the Publiſhing-Houſe, responſible for the Preparation of his Novels, might have urged him to diſtil the Text to a more concentrated Quality. The Story is an Engaging Tale: the Culmination of the Journeys through Life of the three chief <i>Perſonæ</i> of previous Volumes, <i>videlicet</i>, Dr DANIEL WATERHOUSE, the Rogue JACK SHAFTOE, and the Ducheſs ELIZA of ARCACHON-QWLGHM. The Situation of theſe three, and many Others, is in the Year of Grace 1714, and encompaſses the Paſsing of Her Late Majeſty, Queen ANNE, and the Acceſsion to the Throne of Great-Britain and Ireland of the Electoral Prince GEORGE of Hanover. The Chief Strand of the Narrative concerns the Integrity of the Currency of England, as adminiſtered by the Maſter of the Royal Mint, Sir ISAAC NEWTON; we see much of him, and of other Perſonalities, including Baron VON LEIBNITZ, with whom NEWTON engages in lengthy and unexciting Philoſophick Debate, and (more briefly) the Musician Mr HANDEL, who aſsists in the Slaughter of a Rogue, by Uſe of a Violon-Cello as Fatal <i>Inſtrument</i>, in a Thrilling Paſsage. The Atmoſphere of London, Hanover, and other Locations of the Era is conveyed to the Reader with Conviction. But I wiſhed it had not been ſo <i>long</i>.<br /><br />It is no doubt the Caſe, that this Book will be bought - indeed, has already been bought - by thoſe Readers whoſe Habit it is, to peruſe Works of that Genre known to ſome as <i>Scientifick-Phantaſy</i>. Yet (ſaving one Perſonality, barely mentioned in this Volume, who may be an Immortal, though moſt unlike the <i>Struldbrugs</i> encountered by GULLIVER in Dr SWIFT's Tale of his Voyage from Laputa to Japan) there is naught here that is Phantastickal, or reliant on counter-factual Advances in the Technologickal Arts. My own Belief is that the late Mr KNIGHT hit the Nail upon the Head, when he ſurmiſ'd, that the <i>Scientifick-Phantaſy</i> Genre is "what we point to, when we ſay it". I point to this Book, and its two Fellows in the Baroque-Trilogy, and <b>Cryptonomicon</b> which though written earlier is ſet two and a half Centuries later, and I ſay that I include them in that Genre. Does any-body diſagree?</span>
  <a data-text-id="3460762012627007586" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_116631474" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/116631474" />
    <a title="Melissa Rudder" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/824216-melissa-rudder"><img alt="Melissa Rudder" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1201213962p2/824216.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/116631474?book_show_action=true">Aug 13, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melissa Rudder" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melissa Rudder" href="/user/show/824216-melissa-rudder">Melissa Rudder</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer116631474" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13914701460395967044">The final book in Neal Stephenson's <i>The Baroque Cycle</i>, <i>The System of the World</i>, did exactly what the conclusion of a long complex tale, inhabited by a lively cast of characters across five continents, should do: it dazzled its reader with a seemingly unending parade of dramatic climaxes, facilitated by the carefully interwoven tales of seemingly disparate individuals.<br/><br/>My usual complaint about Stephenson's detail-driven writing does not apply to <i>The System of the World</i>. Perhaps the first two insta</span>
  <span id="freeText13914701460395967044" style="display:none">The final book in Neal Stephenson's <i>The Baroque Cycle</i>, <i>The System of the World</i>, did exactly what the conclusion of a long complex tale, inhabited by a lively cast of characters across five continents, should do: it dazzled its reader with a seemingly unending parade of dramatic climaxes, facilitated by the carefully interwoven tales of seemingly disparate individuals.<br /><br />My usual complaint about Stephenson's detail-driven writing does not apply to <i>The System of the World</i>. Perhaps the first two installments were the careful climb to a colossal peak--a climb punctuated by a few enjoyable bumps and turns--so that the final installment could be a thrilling ride through unpredictable twists and the sorts of jolts that make the reader catch his/her breath. Because The System of the World, in spite of its very boring-sounding (but thematically appropriate) title, is really an action-packed thrill ride. (Pardon my abrupt metaphor shift. Wait for it...) The book got very exciting. I do not exaggerate when I tell you that there were moments when I nearly jumped out of my seat like a fan at a Superbowl party, ready to exchange high fives or expletives with the person beside me. It was very disappointing when, at those moments, the only one beside me was my husband, who wasn't watching the game. And it's pointless for me to relate what we all know: just as watching the instant replay of the game-winning touchdown can't produce the excitement of sitting there tensely through every tedious offensive drive before it and THEN watching the receiver just barely keep his toes in the zone, so were my choppy and incomplete explanations of <i>x</i> killing <i>y</i> or <i>b</i> discovering <i>c</i> insufficient to earn me a fellow celebrant. My only recourse was to send excited IMs to my brother, who had read the book, and rush back into reading.<br /><br />I have yet to decide if I'm entirely content with the amount of closure <i>The System of the World</i> provides. It does a somewhat elementary series of the "Where are they now?" chapters to supply the reader with sufficient information about the welfare (or unwelfare) of its sprawling cast, but I would have appreciated a bit more emotion. Although perhaps that wasn't Stephenson's intent. Because by the end of the cycle, I felt a bit like Daniel, the Puritan turned natural philosopher turned courtier turned eccentrically wise and baffling elder: exhausted by the journey and unsure about the world it's produced: a world made smaller by far-reaching ships, far-sighted lenses, and far more powerful engines, in which polarizing issues like abolition, international relations, the growth of public media, and fiat currency only seem to be growing larger.<br /><br />"All human stories are in some sense repetitious, if you boil them down so far. Yet people fall in love... With a <i>particular</i> man or woman, and no one else. Or a woman will have a baby, and love that baby forever... no matter how similar its tale might seem to those of other babies."<br /><br />"There is no <i>sameness</i>. If you looked down upon the world from above, like an albatross, you might phant'sy there was some sameness among the people crowding the land below you. But we are not albatrosses, we see the world from ground level, from within our own bodies, through our own eyes, each with our own frame of reference, which changes as we move about and as others move about us."<br /><br />"There is nothing quite so civilized as to be recognized in public places as the author of books no one has read."<br /><br />"If a want of hope made men desperate, a surfeit of it made them stupid in a wholly other way."<br /><br />"They are all individual souls. This Mobb is a fabrication of minds to lazy to treat them as such."<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13914701460395967044" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_280343718" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/280343718" />
    <a title="Larou" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7821634-larou"><img alt="Larou" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1329869108p2/7821634.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/280343718?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Larou" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Larou" href="/user/show/7821634-larou">Larou</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099463368" href="/book/show/920429.The_System_of_the_World">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7821634-larou?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer280343718" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16160246106508012455">My favourite way of describing Neal Stephenson as an author is that his ambition vastly outstrips his talent; and the <i>Baroque Cycle</i> is a good point in case, I think. It is fairly obvious what he wanted to do here (mainly because Pynchon already did it before him) and it is even more blatantly obvious that this is not the chef-d’oeuvre describing the emergence of an age and short-circuiting that age with our present time that Stephenson wants it to be.<br/><br/>The first novel, <i>Quicksilver</i> had three protag</span>
  <span id="freeText16160246106508012455" style="display:none">My favourite way of describing Neal Stephenson as an author is that his ambition vastly outstrips his talent; and the <i>Baroque Cycle</i> is a good point in case, I think. It is fairly obvious what he wanted to do here (mainly because Pynchon already did it before him) and it is even more blatantly obvious that this is not the chef-d’oeuvre describing the emergence of an age and short-circuiting that age with our present time that Stephenson wants it to be.<br /><br />The first novel, <i>Quicksilver</i> had three protagonists, the second, <i>The Confusion</i>, had two of those, Jack and Eliza, with Daniel being mostly relegated to the background; so it is probably no great surprise that in <i>The System of the World</i> we see Daniel take center stage again, with Jack and Eliza moved to the wings. Also, this third novel takes almost exclusively part in England (and most of that in London – as world-roaming as <i>The Confusion</i> was, so confined is <i>The System of the World</i>), and generally this is by far the most focused novel of the <i>Baroque Cycle</i>, one could almost call it tightly constructed. But only almost, as this probably would just not be Stephenson if he would not go on long tangents at every occasion that offers itself, culminating towards the end of the novel in a moment-by-moment description of the “Trial of the Pyx” (basically, a test of the validity of British coinage) that rambles on and on and on over hundreds of pages (felt pages – actually it’s more like several dozen, but still absurdly long).<br /> <br />There also is some mumbling about the threatening chaos of quicksilver being contained into a solid system of the world – a weak and totally unconvincing bit of legerdemain to make readers believe there is some kind of Deeper Meaning at work in the <i>Baroque Cycle</i> rather than a random agglomeration of pointless facts by which of course nobody is taken in. The thing is that you just might get away with piling up heaps of facts and pieces of information in a non-fiction work, but if you want your text to work as a novel, you need to somehow connect that facts in a way that infuses them with significance – take a look at <i>Moby Dick</i> if you want to see how it’s done properly, or <i>Gravity’s Rainbow</i> (or really anything by Thomas Pynchon who is the supreme master of turning facts into metaphor). Neal Stephenson, on the other hand, just keeps shovelling facts, facts and even more facts into his novels in the hope that they’ll magically cohere into something meaningful – which of course they don’t. At best, the facts are curious in interesting in themselves, at worst they’re just a heap of boring pedantry that – except for the, in this case really minor, difference of their being historical rather than made up – could have comfortably fitted in any of Robert Jordan’s <i>Wheel of Time</i> novels and that only distracts from what remains at heart a rip-roaring adventure story.<br /> <br />Thankfully, that heart beats strong enough in <i>The System of the World</i> to make itself felt through all the intellectual waste Stephenson piles on it, and its rhythm is compelling enough to keep the reader turning the pages even when they are filled with tedious descriptions of irrelevant detail. This third novel of the <i>Baroque Cycle</i> is to my taste at least the most entertaining, with two major struggles driving the plot forward – the rupture between Isaac Newton and Gottfried von Leibniz about the authorship of the calculus which Daniel tries to mediate on one hand, and the struggle between Master of the Mint Newton and master forger Jack Shaftoe in wich Daniel also is involved. It is mainly the second one (no surprise, as Jack plays a central part) which keeps things going and the reader interested as Daniel first hunts down the forger with a group of unlikely investigators (most of which turn out to have – at least! – a double agenda) and then once again becomes a mediator trying to unite the opposing factions in a common purpose. We get a big heist (targeting the tower), a duel (with cannons), a wild chase (with coaches) and quite a few colourful and exciting things more.<br /> <br />Summing up (or well, repeating my sermon for the umpteenth time), <i>The Baroque Cycle</i> could have been such a wonderful book if it wasn’t for Neal Stephenson’s delusions of grandeur. Someone really should rescue the fun adventure novel hidden in the trilogy by pulling an S. Morgenstern on Stephenson and make an abridgement with just the good parts.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16160246106508012455" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
