<div id="review_98424704" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/98424704" />
    <a title="Ryan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1500718-ryan"><img alt="Ryan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1238846167p2/1500718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/98424704?book_show_action=true">Apr 14, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ryan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ryan" href="/user/show/1500718-ryan">Ryan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099463368" href="/book/show/920429.The_System_of_the_World">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer98424704" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12687939052565035009">The <b>Baroque Cycle</b> as a whole takes a great deal of time to read, and I think I've come to somehow identify with the series as a result. Now I find myself torn between an urge to share this series with everyone and to keep it to myself so that it's not cheapened by becoming a 30 second talk piece on <i>The View</i> (apologies to <i>The View</i>). I would also be pleased if these books were never turned into a film or television series, though I would of course have to see it if they were. Although I am normall</span>
  <span id="freeText12687939052565035009" style="display:none">The <b>Baroque Cycle</b> as a whole takes a great deal of time to read, and I think I've come to somehow identify with the series as a result. Now I find myself torn between an urge to share this series with everyone and to keep it to myself so that it's not cheapened by becoming a 30 second talk piece on <i>The View</i> (apologies to <i>The View</i>). I would also be pleased if these books were never turned into a film or television series, though I would of course have to see it if they were. Although I am normally unconcerned with whether a novel will be remembered in the future, I would be satisfied if <b>The Baroque Cycle</b> is remembered as one of the greatest series to be published at the start of the 21st century.<br /><br />And perhaps it will be. For any historical fiction fan, this trilogy will surely be a rare treat, and Stephenson does a fine job of tying together events that happened in the 1660s in <i>Quicksilver</i> (particularly the Scientific Revolution) with the plot of <i>The System of the World</i>. In <i>The Confusion</i> (my favorite installment), Jack took us around the world while Eliza roamed about France and Germany. Now, we have returned to London and its characters, including Newton, Liebniz, and Daniel Waterhouse. Even Robert Hooke returns, if only in a series of notes. Perhaps this return to beginnings explains why so many readers report that they intend to reread the series after they have finished <i>The System of the World</i>.<br /><br />The voice that Stephenson adopts for this sprawling series may throw some readers off. In a series of novels of about 900 pages each, we might expect Stephenson to get to the point -- that he should write in a way that is clear and concise. He never does. Whenever a similitude can be employed in great detail, it is. However, this is exactly what makes this book a joy to read.<br /><br /><i>The System of the World</i> is the final installment in Neal Stephenon's <b>Baroque Cycle</b>. It is also a way of understanding the Western World that began to emerge during the Enlightenment. I never realized it before, but a lot of authors set their historical fiction in the Victorian Era. I would like to see more fiction published about this time period, which, frankly, is just more fun. Then again, perhaps I'd prefer that it remains with just me and this excellent collection of books, <b>The Baroque Cycle</b>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12687939052565035009" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_69903082" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/69903082" />
    <a title="Kristine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/831094-kristine"><img alt="Kristine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1258476728p2/831094.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/69903082?book_show_action=true">Sep 03, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kristine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kristine" href="/user/show/831094-kristine">Kristine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer69903082" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4814860449698635453">In Quicksilver, the first book of the Baroque cycle, it isn't obvious where Stephenson is going. That book is an enjoyable read, to be sure, but I never would have guessed Stephenson's ambition with these novels is to explain how the world we live today came about, where the scientific method rules rather than alchemy, and where money is completely interchangeable, and where finance...well, perhaps that hasn't changed so much, but anyway, where the world we live in came from. More than a simple </span>
  <span id="freeText4814860449698635453" style="display:none">In Quicksilver, the first book of the Baroque cycle, it isn't obvious where Stephenson is going. That book is an enjoyable read, to be sure, but I never would have guessed Stephenson's ambition with these novels is to explain how the world we live today came about, where the scientific method rules rather than alchemy, and where money is completely interchangeable, and where finance...well, perhaps that hasn't changed so much, but anyway, where the world we live in came from. More than a simple historical fiction, this trilogy is a fun and thought-provoking portrayal of a changing world. Technologically, the past century or so has been a period of great change, but there's a case to be made that the philosophical changes portrayed in the Baroque Cycle are as significant, and perhaps harder won (as changing minds is much, much harder than introducing new technology). <br /><br />There are some slow parts of the books, typically revolving around world-building. Stephenson's descriptions frequently include quite prominently excrement and disease, though that's probably a relatively accurate description of life in a city of that era. The climax of The System of the World is a confrontation between Leibnitz and Newton, in which the two of them discuss, for several pages, the impact of science on the world and, in particular, on belief in God. I found the argument fascinating (probably because I'm a scientist and religious and think about these things myself), not only for the philosophy, but also because Stephenson does such a great job portraying the way these two scientists thought.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4814860449698635453" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_44409406" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/44409406" />
    <a title="Alec" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/857630-alec"><img alt="Alec" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1201754496p2/857630.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/44409406?book_show_action=true">Jan 26, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alec" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alec" href="/user/show/857630-alec">Alec</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer44409406" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5154088071082331902">well, I like Neal, but like most of his stuff, all three books in this cycle could have used a better editor. The mere fact that I read all 2736 pages is a testament to his story telling, but I mean come on at least 1432 pages detailed 18th century architecture and fashion. That level of detail is endearing when he is talking code-breaking or operating systems, but the discussion of periwigs lacks glamor.<br/><br/>His characters on many levels are extremely profound and complex, except when it comes to th</span>
  <span id="freeText5154088071082331902" style="display:none">well, I like Neal, but like most of his stuff, all three books in this cycle could have used a better editor. The mere fact that I read all 2736 pages is a testament to his story telling, but I mean come on at least 1432 pages detailed 18th century architecture and fashion. That level of detail is endearing when he is talking code-breaking or operating systems, but the discussion of periwigs lacks glamor.<br /><br />His characters on many levels are extremely profound and complex, except when it comes to their personal relationships. Relationships tend to occur based solely on physical proximity and the authors need to move the story along. This is most evident in the scene in which the protagonist, Daniel is summoned to his best-friend and benefactors house only to find him dead. He died in a sex-bender with his much younger wife. Daniel upon physically moving the corpse is suddenly and inexplicably seduced by the now-widowed younger wife who, overcome with grief at having killed her former husband resumes with Daniel in the <b> same </b> bed. <br /><br />I liked this series and continue to like this author, but this is definitely not on the re-read list.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5154088071082331902" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText15388177534508457968" style="display:none"><p><strong>I was tricked into reading this</strong>, but I'm glad because why else would I have started in on this 2700 page trilogy? Years ago Neal Stephenson intrigued and thrilled me with his cyber-punk classic "Snowcrash" so that I could see where he was going with "Diamond Age" a neo-victorian culture in an incredibly futuristic world. By the time I read "Cryptonomicon" I had enough trust in him as an author to take me through a lot of reading involving multiple characters and time periods and to know it was going to come together satisfactorily.<br /> <br /></p><br /><p> He goes through a lot of history and technical details in these books but the main story and the excitement is sustained all the way. I can't put it any better than the inside jacket blurb from Entertainment Weekly <strong>"...he might just have created the definitive historical-sci-fi-epic-comedy-punk love story. No easy feat that." </strong><br /></p></span>

<div id="review_111915163" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/111915163" />
    <a title="Amanda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3269646-amanda"><img alt="Amanda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1469570174p2/3269646.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/111915163?book_show_action=true">Jul 16, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amanda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amanda" href="/user/show/3269646-amanda">Amanda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3269646-amanda?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3269646-amanda?shelf=series">series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3269646-amanda?shelf=bought-copy">bought-copy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer111915163" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4262793011557455334">This is the third book in Neal Stephenson's Baroque Cycle - well, the last three books, since Stephenson actually wrote eight books that made up the cycle which were then published to form a trilogy. Here the majority of the action takes place in London, where virtually all of the protagonists we have been following end up bringing the story to a mighty conclusion.<br/><br/>The basic plot is that of a murder mystery, but comprises many other components. Daniel Waterhouse has completed his epic trip back a</span>
  <span id="freeText4262793011557455334" style="display:none">This is the third book in Neal Stephenson's Baroque Cycle - well, the last three books, since Stephenson actually wrote eight books that made up the cycle which were then published to form a trilogy. Here the majority of the action takes place in London, where virtually all of the protagonists we have been following end up bringing the story to a mighty conclusion.<br /><br />The basic plot is that of a murder mystery, but comprises many other components. Daniel Waterhouse has completed his epic trip back across the Atlantic at the urging of Princess Caroline. She wished him to bring about the reconciliation of those two mighty Philosophers Leibniz and Newton. In the process of which he ends up stumbling across Jack's scheme to debase English currency (which he is being blackmailed into by the King of France and the dastardly Edouard de Gex). Trying to summarise the plot - the many strands and the different events - is difficult without having to repeat what happened in earlier books or flick through many pages trying to remind myself of exactly who Saturn was and why the Tsar of Russia made an appearance.<br /><br />The cast of characters is enormous and it can be difficult to keep them separate at times, although our main characters have become extremely three dimensional. Daniel, Eliza (although she makes a small appearance in this volume), Jack, Isaac Newton, Dappa, Bob Shaftoe, Ravenscar, Princess Caroline, Leibniz - all these characters become beloved and it is of interest to see what happens to all of them.<br /><br />The three volumes as a whole - the Baroque Cycle - are a truly amazing achievement. It is nigh on 3000 pages dense with facts, with ideas, with characters, with exciting escapes and political machinations. We are shown the beginnings of the world system that we know today - with law enforcement, political parties (Whigs and Tories), real estate and, of course, currency. Either this was written as a fact or Stephenson came up with an extremely clever idea in that currency is called such because of the current of money flowing into London, in this case. There are many such moments during all three books, where you marvel at the level of research and detail that has gone into every element of the story.<br /><br />It is interesting that these books are almost always shelved in the fantasy/sci fi section but, barring the presence of Enoch Root and his little procedure (I shall not say more, for fear of spoiling certain things!) they are more historical in nature.<br /><br />One of my disappointments in this and the previous books is the pacing - we can go from thrilling page-turning events into a deep philosophical discourse and this can make the reader grind to a halt. Despite the exciting nature of the plot in general, there were times when I felt as though it was a struggle to read any further, and this is a sad fact when considering that this should be a series read by everyone. It is a classic in the making - or would be, barring the slow and turgid prose at times. Having said that, it didn't do Tolkien any harm and some people may, in fact, find this one of the charming aspects of Stephenson's writing.<br /><br />I am extremely glad that I read this series, but I shall not be embarking on a re-read for many, many years - if at all. However, I do have the notion that the characters and events will niggle and stay with me - the mark of a book that has had a big effect on me. This should have been a five star experience, but I keep it to four stars purely because of the difficulty of the reading. Recommended (with reservations!) </span>
  <a data-text-id="4262793011557455334" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_182505175" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/182505175" />
    <a title="Josh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/844382-josh"><img alt="Josh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452975129p2/844382.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/182505175?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josh" href="/user/show/844382-josh">Josh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/844382-josh?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/844382-josh?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer182505175" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16662574616002969183">Baroque Summer 2011 finally ends! In February 2012!<br/><br/>It's probably best not to think of these books as three huge tomes, but instead like a longer series of eight normal-sized novels (which the three are divided up into, with the complication that nos. 4 and 5 are told in parallel) or like eight seasons of some well-produced TV show. I wish it had been eight volumes, actually. I never would have been stupid enough to try reading an octology straight through. <br/><br/>Like anything that long, there were gre</span>
  <span id="freeText16662574616002969183" style="display:none">Baroque Summer 2011 finally ends! In February 2012!<br /><br />It's probably best not to think of these books as three huge tomes, but instead like a longer series of eight normal-sized novels (which the three are divided up into, with the complication that nos. 4 and 5 are told in parallel) or like eight seasons of some well-produced TV show. I wish it had been eight volumes, actually. I never would have been stupid enough to try reading an octology straight through. <br /><br />Like anything that long, there were great stretches and slogs. I remember some portions vividly. There are some classic Stephensonian digressions that left me in awe of his genius and affirmed his status as my favorite author. Other times I simply had to put these books down just out of weariness. Seemed more appealing to read something else for a bit rather than get pounded with dozens of pages of fictional third-hand political intrigue, especially knowing I had hundreds of pages to go before there would be any resolution. <br /><br />If I'm rating Stephenson's accomplishment in generating 2700 pages of consistently outstanding prose and wit, I give it 10 stars out of 5. The reality is I'm rating the experience of reading these books, and I'm going to settle on 3 out of 5 for the third volume. I just finished this book and couldn't tell you what happened in the last 900 pages. It must have been a lot, right? Why do I remember the other two volumes more vividly? Should I have put this one off for a few years or taken more breaks during reading? Maybe, but that's the problem right there. I think ultimately, the experience here pales in comparison to his other work.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16662574616002969183" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7672937632840189811">After nearly three long years of reading these books by Neal Stephenson I have finally completed the final volume. The System of the World contains the final three novels in his huge Baroque Cycle, a “project” read that I began back in 2008. This volume contains these three novels: Solomon's Gold, Currency, and The System of the World. All told there are nearly 3000 pages of historical fiction, historical fact, irreverent humor, and a bit of science fiction thrown in.<br/> <br/>I don't have too much to sa</span>
  <span id="freeText7672937632840189811" style="display:none">After nearly three long years of reading these books by Neal Stephenson I have finally completed the final volume. The System of the World contains the final three novels in his huge Baroque Cycle, a “project” read that I began back in 2008. This volume contains these three novels: Solomon's Gold, Currency, and The System of the World. All told there are nearly 3000 pages of historical fiction, historical fact, irreverent humor, and a bit of science fiction thrown in.<br /> <br />I don't have too much to say about the story itself as there is just so much here. Lots of main characters and hundreds of lesser characters, combined with a verbose hi-brow style of writing make these books a viable read only for those who really want to tackle a challenge. Neal Stephenson is one incredibly intelligent human being and he allows his intellect to permeate his prose to such an extent that large parts of these books are almost unreadable. I plowed through them as a "project." That is, I work on them in between other reading with the ultimate goal of just getting through them. I am also a completist and so refused to give up on these books even though I see by reviews that many others did indeed give up on them. I didn't start out planning these as a project but after I got through about 100 pages of the first one, I decided I just couldn't read them in the normal fashion. I would have dumped them. I must say I'm happy that I made it through them but, at the same time, I won't be taking them with me to that proverbial deserted island.</span>

  <span id="freeText9567192781505465477" style="display:none">Well that last book of the trilogy seemed to take quite a long time, but it was a satisfying ending to the Baroque Cycle. I am actually sad to be finished reading about these characters and will be picking up Cryptonomicon at some point to see how their ancestors fare in the mind of Setphenson.<br /><br />This book was a large discourse on the creation of the money system focused in England. It was actually fascinating from an economic point of view. I also love throughout all of the books how Stephenson throws in tidbits about word origins.<br /><br />My only complaint about this particular book is that I was hoping that more of the story would focus on Jack Shaftoe. The plot focused around his exploits, but most of it was not told from his point of view. As a whole, I enjoyed the second book the most as they travel the world, and I liked the cast of characters best that were on the Minerva throughout the second book.<br /><br />Outstanding series of books. It is hard to say who I would recommend this too because it is written in such a unique way. There are some long stretches where the action doesn't move at all, but from a historical inquiry point of view, I found these conversation fascinating. Great stuff and a trilogy I will read again in the future.</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1174172243" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1174172243" />
    <a title="Pete Marchetto" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28932915-pete-marchetto"><img alt="Pete Marchetto" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442968150p2/28932915.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1174172243?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pete Marchetto" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pete Marchetto" href="/user/show/28932915-pete-marchetto">Pete Marchetto</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1174172243" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7726314190748128550">The temptation to two-star this work out of spite was immense, but let's accept it for what it is. A well-written book by a brilliant writer which proves, nonetheless, disappointing.<br/><br/>Stephenson as an excellent but disappointing writer has been the theme of my reviews since - oh, so long ago, my darlings - I began reading him first with the Cryptonomicon and then, in its immediate wake, (or 'aftermath' may here be a better word), headed down the difficult road of reading all three volumes of his B</span>
  <span id="freeText7726314190748128550" style="display:none">The temptation to two-star this work out of spite was immense, but let's accept it for what it is. A well-written book by a brilliant writer which proves, nonetheless, disappointing.<br /><br />Stephenson as an excellent but disappointing writer has been the theme of my reviews since - oh, so long ago, my darlings - I began reading him first with the Cryptonomicon and then, in its immediate wake, (or 'aftermath' may here be a better word), headed down the difficult road of reading all three volumes of his Baroque Cycle of which this, thankfully, is the last.<br /><br />In common with its predecessors it is a massive tome, effectively three novels: 'Solomon's Gold'; 'Currency'; and 'The System of the World'. The period is that of 18th-century England, (for the most part), the early Enlightenment period, and with characters touching upon science, philosophy, and politics, it is historically accurate, albeit with some considerable license taken along the way. Given its themes, many of the characters are known to history - Isaac Newton most notably, of whom more later - and the central character through whose eyes we see most of the events is, as in the preceding two novels, one Daniel Waterhouse who qualifies both as a philosopher and as a politician.<br /><br />Before I launch into my criticism, which is going to require at least one more cup of coffee and several cigarettes, it's worth noting that I was led to read 'Cryptonomicon' followed by the Baroque Cycle by another reviewer. Should you be inclined to read them all, I would suggest reversing that. The characters in the Baroque Cycle are the ancestors of those in the 'Cryptonomicon' and, though Stephenson himself in one of his manifestations of the pointlessly annoying doesn't tend to stick with the chronology, I wish I had taken advantage of that fact by at least tackling them in the appropriate order. <br /><br />Right, coffee... cigarette... and perhaps some preparatory shouting at the cat...<br /><br />...<br /><br />... and we're off. <br /><br />Want to know the plot? Well, largely, forget it. To my certain memory I started reading this volume when dinosaurs roamed the earth, began the entire Baroque Cycle when the sun was nothing more than a ball of gas still coalescing and yet to ignite, and the Cryptonomicon was tackled in that timeless zone of non-existence just before the Big Bang. It is not that I've failed to tackle long works before. Heavens, my dears, I've read Proust and, unlike most people, I didn't stop with the petite madeleine and then wander around talking about it at great length in an effort to kid everyone I'd read the whole thing. Well okay, Proust is a poor example, that was turgid, but I managed Ayn Rand's 'Atlas Shrugged' and, except for the horrendous political diatribe section, it kept my attention as did Vikram Seth's 'A Suitable Boy', one of the best novels I've ever read, so I can do it my dears, I can do it.<br /><br />The problem with Stephenson is you get the idea he doesn't really want you to, or at least couldn't care less if you do or you don't. <br /><br />If there were an Excellent Writers Who Make Strange And Bloody Annoying Choices Award, I would nominate Stephenson. If I could write like him I wouldn't be talking about piffly things like coffee and cigarettes, I'd be stunning you with my erudition, my intellect and, at times, with a sly, wry humour that would leave you if not stunned, then at least considering the possibility of falling to your knees before me in order to pay homage. Beyond that, he can write intelligent adventure scenes in such a way that you can get into the whole 'Boys Own' mindset without ever feeling you've sacrificed your higher faculties in order to do so. The man is an excellent writer, no doubt about it.<br /><br />However, the work is disordered chronologically across the three volumes, flopping you around the years between the two-to-three novel-length works that are put together to make up each volume, and even within them, (though Stephenson appeared to have grasped the conception of chronology by this, the final volume).<br /><br />Chronology is not Stephenson's only poor choice. To read Stephenson is to submerge oneself into a boundless ocean all-but devoid of islands. If this sounds like a compliment, try swimming the Pacific. I'll give you some parentheses.<br /><br />...<br /><br />Back? Done it? No fun, was it? Well, that's the Baroque Cycle for you - wave after featureless wave after featureless wave of it all coming at you and forgotten in the passing as you try to struggle your way through the next one. I didn't know where I was half the time. I lost the plot, lost interest in the characters, didn't even know who or what I should be interested in given it may return later and prove significant rather than being some pointless transgression and so, when events and characters did prove to be important, I was lost all over again.<br /><br />I say all-but devoid of islands. Fortunately, there are sections, chapters, developments which provide not only relief in all this interminable swimming, but also underline what this work could have been had Stephenson not made all his strange choices. Doubtless its these Stephenson's fans remember because truly, they are worth remembering, they are more than mere respite from the endless waves. In these scenes - some conversational, some adventurous, some revolving around some intrigue - I was left with the feeling of 'set pieces' entire unto themselves, divorced from the waves around them. The sea may be their broader setting, but these islands are packed with coconuts and monkeys worthy of exploration while the sea itself most assuredly isn't. Unfortunately, these islands aren't worth all the swimming between them. Stephenson's a good writer, but he's not [em]that[/em] good. I doubt anyone could be.<br /><br />Even here, however, Stephenson once again proves himself the master of the poor choice. Consider - classic Stephenson - two poor choices right at the end of the volume which is, remember, the final volume of three, all exceptionally long. Stephenson, quite rightly, allows two of our loose ends amongst many to be tied up with sufficient drama. Indeed, he interweaves them towards the close of the book, and I found myself hastening through each chapter in my desire to see what was happening in the other scene. So it is that we approach the long-awaited climaxes... and in both instances, one with the second-hand reporting of an observer in a few paragraphs, the other with barely a word, the climax itself comes as a wet lettuce. Splat. That's it. That's your lot. Expected drums and cannons? Forget it. I mean, for heaven's sake, why? It's as if Shakespeare had ended 'Romeo and Juliet' not with its closing scenes, but substituting them with 'PS. It didn't work out in the end and they both died anyway.'<br /><br />Such lost opportunities abound in the cycle. Again and again we encounter a familiar character in an unexpected situation and, after a few pages, Stephenson tells us something really great and exciting happened to put them there, but he couldn't be bothered to write it all down. Well, he doesn't actually say that, but that's what I read. Entire adventures are lost in that fashion. For heaven's sake, why? Weren't those the perfect opportunity for a few less waves and a few more islands?<br /><br />The poor choices make for poor characterisation. After a while you just get bored with these people for the most part. I have listened to a passably-dry thirty-minute lecture on Sir Isaac Newton that had me more captivated by the man than I was at any point in the Baroque Cycle and I'm sorry, but that is quite literally true.<br /><br />Which brings us, finally, to Enoch Root. At this point in the review, were I a Stephenson aficionado, I would go glassy-eyed and assume a look of transcendence. Enoch Root is Stephenson's Gandalf. He is either extraordinarily long-lived - his turning up in the 'Cryptonomicon' set some three-hundred years later rather underlines the point - or is actually immortal, though Stephenson never tells us which. And that's symptomatic of the problem. Stephenson tells us pretty much nothing about Root at all. I get the feeling that, somehow, the spirit of Enoch Root is supposed to hang over the Baroque Cycle and 'Cryptonomicon' both, but in reality he's like one of those characters in a play who is listed as 'Second messenger' or 'Cleaning lady'. On the very rare occasion he makes an actual appearance he doesn't seem to do much of anything at all - certainly nothing that couldn't be done my 'Second messenger' or 'Cleaning lady'. That he is occasionally referenced by one of the central characters in awed tones doesn't rescue him from his deserved obscurity, and the one time he's given the story - right at the very beginning of the cycle - he doesn't come across as anything much at all. Later we find out that we've been viewing the world through the eyes of an immortal, but I'm blowed if I could see the kind of perspective I would expect to see flowing out of the mind of such a being. He may as well have been anyone. It's as if Stephenson felt he had to have this bloke just hanging around, even though he didn't belong in a largely realistic and historical novel, out of some strange obligation. Yes, alchemy is one of the themes of the work, but until near the very end it may be readily dismissed as nothing more than a preoccupation of some of the characters given their timezone. Only at the very end does Stephenson say 'Actually, it works', and then so briefly he may as well not have said it at all.<br /><br />It may be that, at some time in the future, some editor may come along and prevail upon Stephenson to permit the cycle to be edited down into its highlight moments, a not inconsiderable volume of islands connected by bridges as interlinked short stories. Such an editor may even prevail upon Stephenson to write some of the unwritten adventures, or to finish those written properly. Were that to happen it would be a volume I'd highly recommend, but as it stands... no, sorry. As it stands, it's one of those 'If you must' reads. A fuss has been caused, you really ought to find out what the fuss is about. If you do, unless you find something I've managed to miss, prepare yourself for the likelihood of disappointment.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7726314190748128550" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3507458355" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3507458355" />
    <a title="Todd Stockslager" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43477226-todd-stockslager"><img alt="Todd Stockslager" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1432817386p2/43477226.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3507458355?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Todd Stockslager" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Todd Stockslager" href="/user/show/43477226-todd-stockslager">Todd Stockslager</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780060523879" href="/book/show/77588.The_System_of_the_World">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43477226-todd-stockslager?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3507458355" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7683515696512742554">Review title: Sit and watch the river flow<br/><br/> Flow, as both a noun and a verb, is perhaps the best description of this book that closes Stephenson's Baroque Cycle which began with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3389140041' rel='nofollow noopener'>Quicksilver</a> and continued with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3487298763 ' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Confusion</a>. "Currency", as a synonym for money, was to contemporaries of the early 18th century used to describe "the quality that a current has" (p. 336) as in the flow of the River Thames. This currency, this flow of money, the invisible plumbing of the modern marketplace that was just </span>
  <span id="freeText7683515696512742554" style="display:none">Review title: Sit and watch the river flow<br /><br /> Flow, as both a noun and a verb, is perhaps the best description of this book that closes Stephenson's Baroque Cycle which began with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3389140041" rel="nofollow noopener">Quicksilver</a> and continued with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3487298763 " rel="nofollow noopener">The Confusion</a>. "Currency", as a synonym for money, was to contemporaries of the early 18th century used to describe "the quality that a current has" (p. 336) as in the flow of the River Thames. This currency, this flow of money, the invisible plumbing of the modern marketplace that was just taking shape, would wash away the old world ruled by royalty and land ownership. It was to become The System of the World, the title of this final installment introduced on p. 333. If you've stuck with Stephenson's nearly 3,000 pages of history lesson illustrated and enhanced with fictional characters to smooth the digital bits of history into an analog flow of fiction, then you have had the perfect seat to sit and watch this river flow. <br /><br />In the run up to the royal transition amidst impending civil war between Tories backing the monarchy and Whigs supporting the new economic System, Tory leader Bolingbrook asks:<br /><blockquote>"The question is, shall we be ruled by Money, and the Mobb-which are one and the same to me, as neither serves any fixed principle-or by one who serves a higher good? That is the point of Royalty, Roger."<br /><br />Roger [Comstock] paused. "'Tis an attractive prospect," he said. "And I do understand, Henry. We are at a fork in the road just now. One way takes us to a wholly new way of managing human affairs. It is a system I have helped, in my small way, to develop: the Royal Society, the Bank of England, Recoinage, the Whigs, and the Hanoverian Succession are all elements of it. The other way leads us to Versailles, and the rather different scheme that the King of France has got going there." (p. 575)</blockquote><br />As we saw with currency earlier, Stephenson has done his research so he is reflecting contemporary usage, providing a unique insight into the word origins, the language of the time, and how the English language grows and changes to meet our current needs. Here he uses the spelling "Mobb" for the modern meaning of the word mob as a disorganized and unruly group of people, which he says elsewhere is short for "the Mobility" meaning poor common people of no fixed address who would make up such a disorganized and unruly group. Elsewhere he uses the word "a-mazed" to describe one who is lost in a maze, and "con-fused" to describe two things fused together in such a way that they can not be separated (unfused) into their two individual parts. <br /><br />Finally, beginning on p. 676 Stephenson brings together Sir Isaac Newton and Gottfried Liebnitz, the two historical Natural Philosophers who were disputing the primacy of their competing claims to the invention the calculus, to address their deeper philosophical differences on the nature of free will and mind/body materialism. Both were devout Christians, dedicated scientists, and deep thinkers. Stephenson's fictional face to face gives him the chance to summarize and synthesize their writings, some of the most important of the 18th century--and of the 21st. While the sometimes contentious debate continues for about half a dozen pages, Stephenson interrupts it with some fictional nattering and never returns to it. As I said in my previous review these novels are like a course in economic history wrapped in a fictional cover, and this philosophical discussion feels like the economics professor spending 15 minutes at the end of a lecture sketching the outlines of the philosophical arguments and pointing the students to optional reading on the syllabus. It deserves better, although not necessarily more; at nearly 900 pages per volume, this three part course is already more than most students of history, fiction, or philosophy will sit through. <br /><br />And ultimately, after a very good start in <i>Quicksilver</i>, where he frames the forces at play, Stephenson's skill as a novelist don't live up to his reach as a historian. Good fiction is very hard to write well, even (or especially) at this epic level and length, which raises the question whether any novelist could have brought <i>The System</i> to a satisfying conclusion. Stephenson wraps up all of the threads of his fictional characters now so familiar to those who have read this far, but takes far too long to do it. A more succinct writer with a stronger editor could have brought this book to <b>The End</b> in 500 pages, still a substantial commitment of time for the reader and inches for the bookshelf. <br /><br />But I don't want to leave without a nod to the achievement of making the foundations of modern national representative governments based on free economic markets and rooted in the Enlightenment ideals of science, faith, and liberty into any kind of fictional account accessible to the average reader. While the execution doesn't equal the idea the whole way through, the attempt is admirable and worthwhile. I would wholeheartedly recommend readers start the journey with <i>Quicksilver</i> and see if they want to stay the course for full credit. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7683515696512742554" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3507046961" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3507046961" />
    <a title="Jeyavaishnavi Muralikumar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22544278-jeyavaishnavi-muralikumar"><img alt="Jeyavaishnavi Muralikumar" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3507046961?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeyavaishnavi Muralikumar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeyavaishnavi Muralikumar" href="/user/show/22544278-jeyavaishnavi-muralikumar">Jeyavaishnavi Muralikumar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3507046961" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13830540868364367535">As I started on <i>Quicksilver</i>, I decided that this is an author who does not care about catering to a universal audience. Not that anyone should, but these books felt unapologetically esoteric, but not quite, in that they may be understood by anybody but would only appeal to some. As I wrap up <i>The System of the World</i>, I realize that it is not that these books are written for a few - they actually have something in them for everybody, and therefore most will find a lot here that they don't enjoy.<br/><br/>Th</span>
  <span id="freeText13830540868364367535" style="display:none">As I started on <i>Quicksilver</i>, I decided that this is an author who does not care about catering to a universal audience. Not that anyone should, but these books felt unapologetically esoteric, but not quite, in that they may be understood by anybody but would only appeal to some. As I wrap up <i>The System of the World</i>, I realize that it is not that these books are written for a few - they actually have something in them for everybody, and therefore most will find a lot here that they don't enjoy.<br /><br />The genre of this volume is part grown up Secret Seven, part period adventure, part urban heist movie, part attempted romantic drama, and part whatever the hell <i>The Baroque Cycle</i> is. 1714 London is almost a character in itself, introduced and delightfully painted in loving detail. You wouldn't have wanted to live there, but the book does make one feel like they understand the city. And in a break from tradition, this book has an actual climax with build-up and a freaking <i>epilogue</i>. And somewhat unexpectedly, it passes the Bechdel test! I know that I shouldn't really be surprised, given that I've read <i>Seveneves</i>, but the economics of that situation were very different - uteri are appreciating assets in that book, rather than depreciating liabilities. The pacing is remarkable for a Neal Stephenson, and the scene-setting is rather interesting. The political drama, however, can be a tad underwhelming at times, as what would be mind-blowing plot lines to a computer geek reading <i>Quicksilver</i> for the first time seem only, to borrow a phrase from my lord Ravenscar, moderately sophisticated ploys now.<br /><br />Daniel Waterhouse has a very satisfying character arc. He doesn't transform like he did at the end of <i>Quicksilver</i> the book, but rather goes on the journey of a conventional hero-with-a-purpose. He returns to England, which has changed so much that it is no longer just England. His mission is simple: heal the rift between Newton and Leibniz, protect the Whig juncto and see that its goals are accomplished, complete the Logic Mill, set the Industrial Revolution in motion and fulfill the legacies of his father and Bishop Wilkins, all while getting caught up in the organized crime scene. He makes new friends and strengthens old bonds, and carries forward his genuine friendships with the Marquess of Ravenscar and Baron von Leibniz, while managing his on-again-off-again complicated relationship with Newtom. Which, by the way, still makes for a better love story than <i>Twilight</i>. Somehow all of this gets either a satisfying or a realistic ending, sometimes both.<br /><br />Jack Shaftoe has his moments, enough said. My favorite Jack Shaftoe will be the Paris sequences in <i>King of the Vagabonds</i>, but this volume isn't too bad either. Of the new characters, Lord Gy and Sophie of Hanover steal the show. The women don't have foreground roles, given that Eliza is too old to sexualize, Caroline too royal to sexualize, and Sophie both. But they are believable characters of strength, sense, means and agency, the older ones more so. Johann von Hacklheber is essential and entertaining, but nothing out of the ordinary. Of the returning secondary cast, Dappa is the highlight while John Churchill, transmuted into the first Duke of Marlborough, becomes a stranger. As for the Fellows of the Royal Society, Wren has a delightful mention but it's Hooke's shadow that steals the show. Speaking of shadows, the Sun King casts his, but is clearly setting in relevance.<br /><br /><i>The System of the World</i> is a fitting end to a very good historic fiction series, even though said series is not devoid of flaws. There are several callbacks to <i>Quicksilver</i>, and a heartwarming look ahead towards <i>Cryptonomicon</i>. I ended this magnificum opus by tangentially disagreeing with Daniel's sense of peace and achievement, appreciating the extraordinarily well picked context quote from the real John Wilkins, and perusing the Acknowledgements  for further reading, in which the author is humble but conveniently forgets to mention any places or cultures featured in this book that lie outside of Europe. But perhaps I should give him more credit - it has to be harder to access relevant research in those cases.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13830540868364367535" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_583156300" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/583156300" />
    <a title="Lindsay" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1583409-lindsay"><img alt="Lindsay" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1222911391p2/1583409.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/583156300?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lindsay" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lindsay" href="/user/show/1583409-lindsay">Lindsay</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone, provided they've read the other books in the series</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1583409-lindsay?shelf=read-postcollege">read-postcollege</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1583409-lindsay?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1583409-lindsay?shelf=read-for-fun">read-for-fun</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1583409-lindsay?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1583409-lindsay?shelf=alternate-history">alternate-history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1583409-lindsay?shelf=enlightenment">enlightenment</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1583409-lindsay?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer583156300" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1657031306918305245">With this enormous volume, the Baroque Cycle comes to a close. While there is the same kind of speeding up, adding new plot threads and jumping from one set-piece action scene to another that is typical of Stephenson's endings, I thought he actually succeeded at tying everything up in this one. I guess he can do that when he's got an entire epic-length novel in which to end things, as opposed to the fifty pages or so he tends to devote to endings in his stand-alone novels.<br/><br/>In this volume, unlike </span>
  <span id="freeText1657031306918305245" style="display:none">With this enormous volume, the Baroque Cycle comes to a close. While there is the same kind of speeding up, adding new plot threads and jumping from one set-piece action scene to another that is typical of Stephenson's endings, I thought he actually succeeded at tying everything up in this one. I guess he can do that when he's got an entire epic-length novel in which to end things, as opposed to the fifty pages or so he tends to devote to endings in his stand-alone novels.<br /><br />In this volume, unlike its predecessor, the three books blend seamlessly into one another, and the mega-novel reads just like it had all been written at once. (Maybe it was --- it was published first as a three-in-one volume, and only later were the individual books released.)<br /><br />Daniel Waterhouse, the first of the three protagonists to be introduced in <i>Quicksilver</i>, is also the first to show up here. Having been drawn out of his long retirement in Massachusetts by Enoch Root, to try and mediate Newton and Leibniz's decades-long feud over (nominally) the credit for inventing calculus and (ultimately) differences of opinion on cosmology, he has arrived in England, where he is standing in a field that will be the site of an "Engine for Raising Water by Fire" (i.e., a steam engine). From there, he goes on to London in the company of a mysterious person named Threader, whose innumerable discreet business transactions with various wealthy townsmen make the trip take far longer than it should. Daniel figures out that Threader deals in the newfangled paper money that Daniel finds so baffling. At the end of this long, meandering trip, something explodes near their coach. Daniel recognizes the flame as that produced by phosphorus, which tells him 1) the explosion was not an accident and 2) whoever made the bomb is an Alchemist.<br /><br />This one simple task that Daniel is given --- get Newton and Leibniz to talk to each other and start working together instead of at cross purposes --- mutates into an imposing snarl of "side quests" in RPG parlance: Newton is Master of the Mint, and plagued by a mysterious counterfeiter named Jack the Coiner, whom Daniel ends up helping him hunt down; Leibniz has found a royal patron for his Logic Mill project, and he wants Daniel's help in getting the thing built, as well as giving it raw information to encode. There is also a succession crisis --- Queen Anne is childless, old and sickly, and both her brother James and her cousin George have claimed the throne --- and a criminal investigation for Daniel to embroil himself in.<br /><br />Both of the other protagonists, Jack and Eliza, have entered the story by now: Jack is, of course, Jack the Coiner, working covertly for Louis XIV to undermine Britain's economic power, and Eliza has attached herself to the Hanover court, as she is a close friend of Princess Caroline, whose mother wed George August, who is now one of the claimants to the English throne. Eliza and Daniel are playing for the same team --- Daniel is a Whig, mostly for cultural reasons (he's a Puritan, and there is strong overlap between the Puritans and the Whigs), he's helping Leibniz, whom Eliza also tries to help, and he's helping Newton, whom the Tories are trying to discredit as Master of the Mint. But Jack is working against both of them, which must cause him some internal conflict because he still loves Eliza, though he tries to make himself forget this. <br /><br />Other characters figuring in this volume are Jack's brother Bob Shaftoe, who is still a sergeant in the Queen's (later King's) Own Black Torrent Guards, and who, with his regiment, helps Daniel and Newton raid a castle belonging to a Tory lord where they suspect Jack the Coiner may be hiding, and later takes part in some skirmishes with Tory militiamen. His regiment is also charged with guarding the Pyx, where samples of coins taken from circulation at regular intervals are kept under lock and key, stored until such time as someone high-placed takes it upon himself to have their purity assayed in a Trial of the Pyx. <br /><br />There is also Eliza's handsome and resourceful German-born son, Johann; a Puritan shipbuilder named Nathan Orney, who has some wonderfully arch exchanges with Daniel (they call each other "Brother Nathan" and "Brother Daniel," though their feelings for one another are pretty far from brotherly); the huge, one-armed Russian agent provocateur named Yevgeny; the wily Jesuit priest Edouard de Gex, who is in England supervising Jack's sabotage of the English currency; Dappa, who has left Jack's employ for Eliza's, and who has taken up the pen to write articles condemning slavery; and Charles White, an odious person who serves the Viscount Bolingbroke, leader of the Tory faction, and who decides that Dappa, being a black man in England, must be someone's property, so he might as well be his, Charles White's, property. He has Dappa thrown into jail, from whence he directs all his pamphleteering at White personally. Their feud culminates in what may be the most absurd dueling scene this side of Twain's "The Great French Duel."<br /><br />The ending of the book is truly epic: two climaxes build at once, cutting from one to the other. They have been long in coming: they are the Trial of the Pyx and the execution of Jack the Coiner. Stephenson draws them out, longer I think than any other scene in any of the books. But the drawing-out doesn't feel slow at all; it gives those scenes a sense of grandeur and finality. <br /><br />Another thing I loved about the ending of this book --- which, after the two climaxes play out, consists of a series of epilogues showing where each major character ends up --- was its thematic coherence. Toward the middle of the book the title is explained: Princess Caroline is presiding over the reconciliation of Newton and Leibniz, and she tells them she wants them to work together because she senses that a new System of the World, a more rational one guided by science, technology and commerce, was being born, but that it was a fragile one that would require both of their combined efforts to keep on track. She specifically worried that the flowering of science would lead to a withering of Christianity, and she called on both philosophers to try to forestall that. At the end of the book, Daniel is standing in the middle of a mine that has been pumped dry by one of the new steam engines, and he reflects that the new System has succeeded in displacing the old. Throughout the saga, Daniel and Eliza have been instrumental in bringing it forth: Daniel has furthered the cause of Natural Philosophy, and Eliza has championed commerce. Eliza's anti-slavery labors also fall under the rubric of this new System: as Daniel sees it, the machines the new System enables man to build will do the work that slaves used to do, and will render slavery obsolete as well as morally wrong. (I do not think that this is true; I know that slavery has survived the machine age.) Newton seems to have one foot in the old System and one foot in the new; he's the world's pre-eminent Natural Philosopher, and as Master of the Mint he oversees the rationalization of England's economic system, but his great passion is Alchemy. Indeed, he tells Daniel he only took the Mint position to try and get his hands on the fabled Solomonic gold, the gold suffused with the Philosophic Quintessence that makes it heavier than all other gold and that, distilled, hardens into the Philosopher's Stone that grants eternal life. And Jack seems to be entirely a man of the old System, thriving on chaos and unpredictability. Everything he does --- undermining Newton's coinage, trying to have Daniel, Newton or both of them killed, throwing his lot in with the absolutist King Louis XIV, as opposed to the ever-more-republican English government, stealing the Solomonic gold and unleashing it upon the world --- seems opposed to the forces of Reason and Modernity, except that he also exemplifies some very modern values, like individualism and egalitarianism. As King of the Vagabonds, his command of the Mobb, and his appreciation for the Mobb's power and knowledge of its nature, prefigure the modern era when most countries are democracies. And in that way, Jack comes off as the most forward-looking of the characters, seeing the potential of mere peasants to be political actors when Daniel and Eliza are fixated on Kings, Barons, Dukes, Princes and Princesses. <br /><br />Long story short, Neal Stephenson is a genius. I do not doubt I will revisit this series many times.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1657031306918305245" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2436512970" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2436512970" />
    <a title="Will Peterson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1853260-will-peterson"><img alt="Will Peterson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1559175592p2/1853260.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2436512970?book_show_action=true">Oct 12, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will Peterson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will Peterson" href="/user/show/1853260-will-peterson">Will Peterson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2436512970" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2511255562626658084">I honestly feel sort of shell shocked (in a good way).<br/><br/>It took me 6 months to work my way through these three thousand-page tomes (Quicksilver, The Confusion, The System of the World), but I feel like I've lived a lifetime of experience in that time.<br/><br/>Go into these books by treating them as historical fiction (even if they have elements of other genres). I learned 10x more about the late 1600's by reading these books than all the history classes I've ever taken. Through these books, you will see (o</span>
  <span id="freeText2511255562626658084" style="display:none">I honestly feel sort of shell shocked (in a good way).<br /><br />It took me 6 months to work my way through these three thousand-page tomes (Quicksilver, The Confusion, The System of the World), but I feel like I've lived a lifetime of experience in that time.<br /><br />Go into these books by treating them as historical fiction (even if they have elements of other genres). I learned 10x more about the late 1600's by reading these books than all the history classes I've ever taken. Through these books, you will see (or rather feel) Europe move from medieval times to modern times. In the course of a few decades, the world is modernizing with respect to currency, finance, politics, and technology. And you watch it all happen in what feels like real time.<br /><br />But these aren't history textbooks, they are excellent character driven novels. You get to experience all these changes through amazing relate-able characters. Some of these characters are real historical figures. Many, like Isaac Newton, are main characters that you get to know on a personal level. In fact, the lines between history and fiction are so blurred that I literally have no clue if some of these people and events actually happened. And that's an awesome feeling. <br /><br />I think what leaves me so shell-shocked is the level of research that must have occurred in the writing of these books. Tolkien or Martin have painstaking years of worldbuilding, but all they need is consistency, not accuracy. Neal Stephenson gives the same feeling of depth about the real world. From obscure political rituals in England, to the genealogies of royalty, to the nuances of shipbuilding you get the feeling of immersion with the added fact that these were real places and real people (even if not real events and conversations). There will be passages where you are bored: geographical descriptions, or long-winded monologues about drama in the court of Louis XIV. You'll hear Isaac Newton and Leibniz argue about free will and determinism. But the long stretches of the journey make the final destination that much richer. <br /><br />It's like the Count of Monte Cristo mixed with Robin Hood mixed with A Song of Ice and Fire, and throughout the story Gandalf the Grey is lurking in the periphery of the story to subtly encourage the protagonists towards unseen ends. There's swashbuckling piracy and crazy antics that sometimes border on the absurd, yet feel like they belong. There's heart-wrenching romance, adventures in the jungle, and medieval wars.<br /><br />There are also heavy themes of mathematics, natural sciences, and finance. We get to peer over the shoulders of famous historical figures as they develop telescopes, discover the world of microbiology, explore stock markets and manipulate politicians. We get to feel the rush that it must have been to live in the age when the promise of steam engines and computers could barely be glimpsed on the horizon, when the power of the people was eating away at the power of the absolute Monarch, without knowing what would come next!<br /><br />Reading these books is a long term investment, but the payoff is worth it!<br /><br />Now I need to go decompress. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2511255562626658084" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2777625973?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jerry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jerry" href="/user/show/38672267-jerry">Jerry</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2777625973" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10904431022510716966">This series (Quicksiver, The Confusion, The System of the World) took me a while to get into. But to me, the most fascinating time in history is the period when Europe was colonizing the Americas, because you had a huge clash of 3 continents, and hundreds of cultures and subcultures. <br/><br/>I think too little attention is paid to how events in Europe (wars, succession, economy, religion, scientific thought, the birth of currency, etc.) played themselves out in the slave trade and the theft of the Ameri</span>
  <span id="freeText10904431022510716966" style="display:none">This series (Quicksiver, The Confusion, The System of the World) took me a while to get into. But to me, the most fascinating time in history is the period when Europe was colonizing the Americas, because you had a huge clash of 3 continents, and hundreds of cultures and subcultures. <br /><br />I think too little attention is paid to how events in Europe (wars, succession, economy, religion, scientific thought, the birth of currency, etc.) played themselves out in the slave trade and the theft of the Americas. I ma putting it on my list of books to read if you want to understand that colonization.<br /><br />This series, although fictional, includes hundreds of real people and real events. I have never read anything that gave me such insight into how events in Europe affected the New World. And probably a really accurate portrayal of life, trade with India and Asia, piracy, etc. </span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('MyzI6+UHVHNQiM9AwysHQwCt2uTl64Y2eCDibuTvDD0JCqE5XlmdG/ejeyE03g2c1vI9LCtmCFEjXqTfKaz+pw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ljXQO5uaYxKVsqvk2pIaI4awv5rouruimU/r+1GUTjusE7npIMSqejKZH4UtZxD8UO9YUiY3NcXCMa1KnNe8oQ==')})
  }

Abandonados por la máqu<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText9260530270789620262\" style=\"display:none\">En El Incidente Jesús, Frank Herbert y Bill Ransom describieron el intento de los humanos por colonizar un planeta hostil, Pandora, cuyos océanos estaban habitados en su tiempo por un ser inmenso e inteligente, el varec Avata. Abandonados por la máquina inteligente de la Nave que los llevó hasta Pandora, diezmados por los efectos de una mutación genética inducida deliberadamente, los colonos humanos tuvieron que enfrentarse a una tremenda lucha para sobrevivir, acabando por destruir el planeta ... y descubrir, a sus propias expensas, que el varec regulaba la turbulencia de los océanos. \n\nAhora, las olas han erosionado los continentes hasta el punto de convertir Pandora en una mar inmenso, único y turbulento. Han transcurrido varios siglos y los supervivientes han evolucionado, formando dos facciones separadas, que constituyen casi dos razas distintas: los sirenios, muy avanzados tecnológicamente, que viven en las profundidades marinas harto sofisticadas; y los isleños, casi todos mutados hasta cierto punto, que viven en enormes balsas-ciudades orgánicas, que flotan en los océanos de Pandora, y cuya forma de vida depende de la ingeniería biológica. \n\nLos dos grupos coexisten con grandes dificultades, aunque al iniciarse la novela reina la paz, si bien amenazada por varios factores. Los sirenios tratan de recuperar, orbitando el planeta, los tanques de hibernación abandonados por la Nave, que contienen una gran variedad de vida animal no mutada, así como humanos en animación suspendida, con los que intentan restablecer la vida en las tierras secas de Pandora. Un grupo de sirenios desea, asimismo, disponer de los isleños, a los que consideran algo menos que humanos. Y como resultado de otro proyecto sirenio, el varec, destruido siglos atrás, comienza a revivir. \n\nFrank Herbert es un brillante ecologista y futurista, además del autor de una serie de best-sellers entre los que se cuenta la saga de Dune, uno de los libros más vendidos de toda la historia de la ciencia ficción. Bill Ransom, por su parte, es un poeta nominado para el premio Pulitzer. Ambos unieron sus esfuerzos para crear, a partir de la obra de Herbert Destino: el vacío, una trilogía aclamada en todo el mundo, que reúne los temas de la inteligencia artificial y el ordenador convertido en dios, la conquista de un mundo extraño y peligroso, y la evolución de la humanidad a través de sus propias manipulaciones genéticas. \n\nEl Incidente Jesús, El Efecto Lázaro y El factor ascensión, que han sido calificados como la más emocionante aventura después de Dune, son tres libros que no pueden faltar en ninguna biblioteca.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"9260530270789620262\"       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/545.Neal_Stephenson">About Neal Stephenson</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/545.Neal_Stephenson"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1430920344p3/545.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/545.Neal_Stephenson">Neal Stephenson
</a></div>
<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
21,287
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer16906184076739385170">Neal Stephenson is the author of <i>Reamde, Anathem,</i> and the three-volume historical epic the Baroque Cycle (<i>Quicksilver, The Confusion,</i> and <i>The System of the World</i>), as well as <i>Cryptonomicon, The Diamond Age, Snow Crash,</i> and <i>Zodiac.</i> He lives in Seattle, Washington.</span>

</div>
</div>
